1 of 825 DOCUMENTS


City of Asheville v. State


No. 391PA15


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


794 S.E.2d 759; 2016 N.C. LEXIS 1133


May 17, 2016, Heard in the Supreme Court 
December 21, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: City of Asheville v. State, 777 S.E.2d 92, 2015 N.C. App. LEXIS 823 (N.C. Ct. App., 2015)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-2013 N.C. Sess. Laws 1605, 1618, involuntarily transferring appellant city's water system to a metropolitan district, violated N.C. Const. art. II, § 24 because it was a local law crafted to only apply to the city without explaining why every other city was exempt or why its benefits should not be available to every municipal water system's customers; [2]-The legislation related to the prohibited subject of health and sanitation, contrary to N.C. Const. art. II, § 24(1)(a), because its stated purpose was to provide water and sewer services to affected customers, and the fact that it changed the governance of the city's water system did not remove it from N.C. Const. art. II, § 24(1)(a)'s prohibition, as a local act shifting responsibility for enforcing health and safety regulations from one entity to another clearly related to health and sanitation.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.

 

2 of 825 DOCUMENTS


Town of Boone v. State


No. 93A15-2


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


794 S.E.2d 710; 2016 N.C. LEXIS 1134


March 2, 2016, Heard in the Supreme Court 
December 21, 2016, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected July 12, 2017.


PRIOR HISTORY: Town of Boone v. State, 368 N.C. 252, 2015 N.C. LEXIS 1319 (2015)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The Boone Act, 2013 N.C. Sess. Laws 139, withdrawing a town's extraterritorial jurisdiction was an exercise of the General Assembly's plenary authority to provide for the organization and government and fixing of boundaries under the first clause of N.C. Const. art. VII, § 1, where it restored the local government boundaries within the county, and the General Assembly was the only body politic uniquely qualified to oversee local government and set the jurisdictional lines that divided the town and county; [2]-The limitations imposed by N.C. Const. art. II, § 24, did not apply as this was an action by the General Assembly establishing or modifying the jurisdictional boundaries of local governmental units.


OUTCOME: Decision reversed.

 

3 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Bishop


No. 223PA15


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


368 N.C. 869; 787 S.E.2d 814; 2016 N.C. LEXIS 440; 44 Media L. Rep. 1837


February 17, 2016, Heard in the Supreme Court 
June 10, 2016, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Counsel Amended June 20, 2016.


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Bishop, 774 S.E.2d 337, 2015 N.C. App. LEXIS 522 (N.C. Ct. App., 2015)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The court of appeals erred in upholding defendant's conviction for cyberbullying in violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-458.1(a)(1)(d) (2015) because the statute violated the First Amendment as applied to the states through the Fourteenth Amendment; [2]-The cyberbullying statute restricted speech, not merely non-expressive conduct, that restriction is content based, not content neutral, and the cyberbullying statute was not narrowly tailored to the State's asserted interest in protecting children from the harms of online bullying; [3]-The statute did not embody the least restrictive means of advancing the State's compelling interest in protecting minors from the potential harm because the protection of minors' mental well-being could be a compelling governmental interest, but it was hardly clear that teenagers required protection via the criminal law from online annoyance.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.

 

4 of 825 DOCUMENTS


N.C. Ass'n of Educators v. State


No. 228A15


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


368 N.C. 777; 786 S.E.2d 255; 2016 N.C. LEXIS 314


February 15, 2016, Heard in the Supreme Court 
April 15, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: N.C. Ass'n of Educators, Inc. v. State, 776 S.E.2d 1, 2015 N.C. App. LEXIS 453 (N.C. Ct. App., 2015)



DISPOSITION: MODIFIED AND AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Sections 9.6 and 9.7 of the Current Operations and Capital Improvements Appropriations Act of 2013, 2013 N.C. Sess. Laws 995, were unconstitutional because the repeal of the Career Status Law unlawfully infringed upon the contract rights of those teachers who had already achieved career status in violation of the Contract Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 10; [2]-Retroactively revoking career status from those teachers whose career status rights had already vested deprived career teachers of the promise of continuing employment, as well as the right to a hearing in circumstances in which their shortened contracts could not be renewed; [3]-The teachers relied both on the promise of continued employment as a form of added compensation to supplement their lower salaries and on the benefits of career status when deciding to continue teaching in the public school systems.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

 

5 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Walters


No. 344PA14


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


368 N.C. 749; 782 S.E.2d 505; 2016 N.C. LEXIS 174


February 17, 2016, Heard in the Supreme Court 
March 18, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Walters, 236 N.C. App. 247, 765 S.E.2d 122, 2014 N.C. App. LEXIS 954 (N.C. Ct. App., 2014)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The court of appeals erred in vacating defendant's conviction for first-degree kidnapping because the trial court's disjunctive jury instruction did not violate defendant's constitutional right to be convicted only by the unanimous verdict of a jury in open court, N.C. Const. art. I, § 24; [2]-The trial court properly instructed the jury that it could convict defendant only if it found beyond a reasonable doubt that he had committed one or more specific acts, any of which could establish an essential element of the offense; [3]-It was not necessary for the State to prove, nor for the jury to find, that defendant committed a particular act other than that of confining, restraining, or removing the victim; [4]-Beyond that, defendant's intent or purpose was the focus.


OUTCOME: Decision reversed.

 

6 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. McCrory v. Berger


No. 113A15


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


368 N.C. 633; 781 S.E.2d 248; 2016 N.C. LEXIS 33


June 30, 2015, Heard in the Supreme Court 
January 29, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: McCrory v. Berger, 368 N.C. 253, 769 S.E.2d 629, 2015 N.C. LEXIS 243 (2015)



DISPOSITION: MODIFIED AND AFFIRMED IN PART; VACATED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 130A-309.202(b), 143B-291(a1), and 143B-293.2(a1) (2014) violated the separation of powers clause, N.C. Const. art. I, § 6, because the legislative branch exerted too much control over commissions that had final executive authority and thus, prevented the Governor from performing his express constitutional duty to take care that the laws were faithfully executed, N.C. Const. art. III, § 5(4); [2]-When the General Assembly appointed executive officers that the Governor had little power to remove it could appoint them without the Governor's influence, which left the Governor with little control over the views and priorities of the officers; [3]-When those officers formed a majority on a commission that had the final say on how to execute the laws the General Assembly, not the Governor, could exert most of the control over the executive policy implemented.


OUTCOME: Decision affirmed as modified.

 

7 of 825 DOCUMENTS


Dickson v. Rucho


No. 201PA12-3


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


368 N.C. 481; 781 S.E.2d 404; 2015 N.C. LEXIS 1281


August 31, 2015, Heard in the Supreme Court 
December 18, 2015, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected November 4, 2016.
Modified by, Rehearing denied by Dickson v. Rucho, 368 N.C. 673, 789 S.E.2d 436, 2016 N.C. LEXIS 405 (N.C., Feb. 11, 2016)
Petition for certiorari filed at, 06/30/2016


PRIOR HISTORY: Dickson v. Rucho, 367 N.C. 542, 766 S.E.2d 238, 2014 N.C. LEXIS 1208 (2014)
Dickson v. Rucho, 135 S. Ct. 1843, 191 L. Ed. 2d 719, 2015 U.S. LEXIS 2744 (U.S., 2015)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The redistricting plans did not violate the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment as they did not impermissibly classify individuals based upon their race because, as to the 26 districts drawn to comply with the federal Voting Rights Act of 1965, although the three-judge panel erred when it applied strict scrutiny prematurely, there was a compelling state interest in creating districts that would avoid liability under sections 2 and 5 of the Voting Rights Act, 52 U.S.C.S. §§ 10301 and 10304; and because, as to the remaining challenged districts, the predominant factors in their creation were the traditional and permissible redistricting principles encompassed within the mandatory framework as established by precedents of the United States Supreme Court and the North Carolina Supreme Court.


OUTCOME: Judgment and memorandum of decision affirmed.

State v. Packingham


No. 366PA13


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


368 N.C. 380; 777 S.E.2d 738; 2015 N.C. LEXIS 1061


September 8, 2014, Heard in the Supreme Court 
November 6, 2015, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected March 22, 2016.
US Supreme Court certiorari granted by Packingham v. North Carolina, 137 S. Ct. 368, 196 L. Ed. 2d 283, 2016 U.S. LEXIS 6404 (U.S., Oct. 28, 2016)
Reversed by, Remanded by Packingham v. North Carolina, 137 S. Ct. 1730, 198 L. Ed. 2d 273, 2017 U.S. LEXIS 3871 (U.S., June 19, 2017)
Remanded by State v. Packingham, 2017 N.C. LEXIS 531 (N.C., July 27, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Packingham, 229 N.C. App. 293, 748 S.E.2d 146, 2013 N.C. App. LEXIS 876 (Aug. 20, 2013)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The court of appeals erred in vacating defendant's conviction for accessing a social networking Web site as a registered sex offender in violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-202.5 (2013) on the ground the statute was unconstitutional because it was constitutional on its face and as applied; [2]-The statute was constitutional on its face since it was narrowly tailored to serve a substantial governmental interest, prohibiting registered sex offenders from accessing only Web sites that gave them the opportunity to gather information about minors, and left available ample alternative channels of communication; [3]-Section 14-202.5 was not an unreasonable regulation and was constitutional as applied to defendant because the incidental burden imposed upon him was not greater than necessary to further the governmental interest of protecting children from registered sex offenders.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.

10 of 825 DOCUMENTS


High Point Bank & Trust Co. v. Highmark Props., LLC


No. 8PA14


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


368 N.C. 301; 776 S.E.2d 838; 2015 N.C. LEXIS 936


November 17, 2014, Heard in the Supreme Court 
September 25, 2015, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected March 29, 2017.


PRIOR HISTORY: High Point Bank & Trust Co. v. Highmark Props., LLC, 231 N.C. App. 31, 750 S.E.2d 886, 2013 N.C. App. LEXIS 1240 (N.C. Ct. App., 2013)



DISPOSITION: MODIFIED AND AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-N.C. Gen. Stat. § 45-21.36 establishes an equitable method of calculating the indebtedness, and therefore it is not a defense in the usual sense which can be waived; [2]-The statute simply allows guarantors the right to a judicial method of debt calculation that accounts for the fair market value of the property at the time of a foreclosure sale; [3]-Accordingly, notwithstanding the waiver language contained in the guaranty agreements, guarantors did not waive their right to the protection of the statute; [4]-While the statute is an equitable method of debt calculation as opposed to a traditional defense that is subject to waiver, waiver of this statutory protection as a prerequisite to receipt of a mortgage or as a condition of a guaranty agreement would violate public policy.


OUTCOME: Judgment modified and affirmed.

 

11 of 825 DOCUMENTS


Hart v. State


No. 372A14


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


368 N.C. 122; 774 S.E.2d 281; 2015 N.C. LEXIS 655


February 24, 2015, Heard in the Court 
July 23, 2015, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Hart v. State, 766 S.E.2d 616, 2014 N.C. LEXIS 938 (N.C., 2014)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial court erred in declaring unconstitutional the Opportunity Scholarship Program, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 115C-562.2(a) (2014), which allowed students in lower-income families to receive scholarships from the State to attend private school, because no prohibition in the constitution or in supreme court precedent foreclosed the General Assembly's enactment of the legislation; [2]-N.C. Const. art. IX, § 6 did not prohibit the General Assembly from appropriating tax revenues to the Program because it was funded from general revenues, not from sources of funding that N.C. Const. art. IX, § 6 reserved for public schools; [3]-The appropriations made for the Program were for a public purpose under N.C. Const. art. V, §§ 2(1) and 2(7) because the ultimate beneficiary of providing children to lower-income families additional educational opportunities was the collective citizenry.


OUTCOME: Order reversed.

 

12 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. May


No. 510PA13


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


368 N.C. 112; 772 S.E.2d 458; 2015 N.C. LEXIS 456


November 17, 2014, Heard in the Supreme Court 
June 11, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. May, 749 S.E.2d 483, 2013 N.C. App. LEXIS 1140 (N.C. Ct. App., 2013)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether a trial court's instructions to the jury were improperly coercive. HOLDINGS: [1]-Because defendant failed to raise a contemporaneous objection when the pertinent instructions were given, any error was not preserved. Nonetheless, the appropriate standard of review of defendant's claims under N.C. Const. art. I, § 24 and N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-1235 (2013) was plain error review; [2]-Defendant failed to establish that the trial court plainly erred in its jury instructions because, through the course of three separate instructions, the trial court repeatedly emphasized to the jurors the importance of their individual convictions, while giving instructions that substantially tracked the language of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-1235(b) (2013); [3]-The trial court's instructions did not result in an unconstitutional coercion of a deadlocked jury under N.C. Const. art. I, § 24.


OUTCOME: Judgment of the intermediate appellate court reversed.

 

13 of 825 DOCUMENTS



State v. Grice


No. 501PA12


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


367 N.C. 753; 767 S.E.2d 312; 2015 N.C. LEXIS 69


November 19, 2013, Heard in the Supreme Court 
January 23, 2015, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition denied by Grice v. N.C., 2015 U.S. LEXIS 3938 (U.S., June 15, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Grice, 735 S.E.2d 354, 2012 N.C. App. LEXIS 1316 (N.C. Ct. App., 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Defendant had no privacy interest in the marijuana plants left in plain view of his driveway, where any member of the public coming to his door might have seen them and thus, there could be no search under the Fourth Amendment; [2]-The presence of clearly identifiable contraband 15 yards from the home, in plain view, justified walking further into the curtilage to procure the plants; [3]-The seizure of the plants was also justified by exigent circumstances, as leaving an officer behind to secure the yard could have exposed the officer to unknown danger.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.


 

14 of 825 DOCUMENTS



State v. Banks


No. 90PA13


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


367 N.C. 652; 766 S.E.2d 334; 2014 N.C. LEXIS 961


February 17, 2014, Heard in the Supreme Court 
December 19, 2014, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Counsel Amended January 7, 2015.


PRIOR HISTORY: N.C. Court of Appeals. No. 12-531. Ann Marie Calabria, Judge.
State v. Banks, 736 S.E.2d 843, 2013 N.C. App. LEXIS 131 (N.C. Ct. App., 2013)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether defendant received ineffective assistance of counsel, in violation of U.S. Const. amend. VI and XIV and N.C. Const. art. I, §§ 19 and 23, when his trial counsel failed to object on double jeopardy grounds to defendant's being sentenced by the trial court for both statutory rape and second-degree rape when the convictions were predicated on a single act of sexual intercourse with the victim. HOLDINGS: [1]-Defendant was properly convicted of both statutory rape and second-degree rape committed during a single act of sexual intercourse so that separate punishments for each offense were appropriate. Consequently, defendant could not have been prejudiced by ineffective assistance of counsel when a double jeopardy argument, under U.S. Const. amend. V and N.C. Const. art. I, § 19, would have been unsuccessful at trial.


OUTCOME: Judgment of the intermediate appellate court reversed.


 

15 of 825 DOCUMENTS


Falk v. Fannie Mae


No. 197PA13


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


367 N.C. 594; 766 S.E.2d 271; 2014 N.C. LEXIS 948


October 6, 2014, Heard in the Supreme Court 
December 19, 2014, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Counsel Amended January 7, 2015.


PRIOR HISTORY: N.C. Court of Appeals. No. 12-764. Douglas McCullough, Judge.
Falk v. Mae, 738 S.E.2d 404, 2013 N.C. App. LEXIS 232 (N.C. Ct. App., 2013)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether an irrevocable trust's failure to file an affidavit extending the life of its lien before the foreclosure upon the property by the Federal National Mortgage Association (Fannie Mae) in 2011 undermined the trust's security interest in the property. HOLDINGS: [1]-The trust's note, which was payable on demand, matured on the date of its execution, and, for the purposes of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 45-37(b) (2011), the trust note--and the trust deed that was executed to secure repayment under the note--expired fifteen years after the date of the note's execution; [2]-Fannie Mae, as a qualifying creditor who took its interest in the property from the mortgagor, was able to benefit from § 45-37(b)'s conclusive presumption regarding the life of the lien irrespective of the fact that its interest was recorded and assigned before expiration of the statute's fifteen year period.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.

State v. Bowden


No. 514PA08-3


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


367 N.C. 680; 766 S.E.2d 320; 2014 N.C. LEXIS 954



December 19, 2014, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Counsel Amended January 7, 2015.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Bowden v. North Carolina, 2015 U.S. LEXIS 7702 (U.S., Dec. 7, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY: N.C. Court of Appeals. No. 12-1072. Wanda G. Bryant, Judge.
State v. Bowden, 747 S.E.2d 617, 2013 N.C. App. LEXIS 883 (N.C. Ct. App., 2013)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The lower courts erred in ruling that all of defendant's credits should be applied to his sentence for all purposes, including calculating an unconditional release date, because previous holdings regarding the particular class of inmates that included defendant--whose death sentences had been vacated and replaced with life sentences under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-2 (Supp. 1974)--mandated the conclusion that he remained lawfully incarcerated because defendant remained lawfully incarcerated and was not entitled to release where the nature and severity of his offenses warranting the life sentence remained the same, the DOC retained the same implicit discretion in governing him pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 148-4, 148-11, 148-13 (2013), and had never applied credits towards the calculation of an unconditional release date for such inmates.


OUTCOME: Decision reversed.


 

17 of 825 DOCUMENTS



Dickson v. Rucho


No. 201PA12-2


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


367 N.C. 542; 766 S.E.2d 238; 2014 N.C. LEXIS 1208


January 6, 2014, Heard in the Supreme Court 
December 19, 2014, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected February 5, 2015.
Counsel Amended January 7, 2015.
Vacated by, Remanded by Dickson v. Rucho, 135 S. Ct. 1843, 191 L. Ed. 2d 719, 2015 U.S. LEXIS 2744 (U.S., 2015)
Reaffirmed Dickson v. Rucho, 2015 N.C. LEXIS 1281 (N.C., Dec. 18, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY: Wake County. No. 11CVS16896, 11CVS1690.
Dickson v. Rucho, 2013 NCBC LEXIS 53 (2013)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial court properly found that the General Assembly's redistricting plans satisfied constitutional and statutory requirements because, while the trial court erred in applying strict scrutiny prematurely, the voters would gain nothing on remand where compliance with §§ 2 and 5 of the Voting Rights Act of 1965, 52 U.S.C.S. §§ 10301 and 10304, was a compelling state interest, the General Assembly had a strong evidentiary basis on which to conclude that race-based remedial action was necessary, the redistricting was sufficiently narrowly tailored to advance the state interests, and other factors were considered in addition to race, such as the Whole County Provision of N.C. Const. art. II, §§ 3(3) and 5(3), one-person, one-vote requirements, partisan considerations, population equalization, protecting incumbents, and fashioning more competitive districts.


OUTCOME: Orders affirmed.


 

18 of 825 DOCUMENTS



King v. Town of Chapel Hill


No. 281PA13


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


367 N.C. 400; 758 S.E.2d 364; 2014 N.C. LEXIS 401


March 17, 2014, Heard in the Supreme Court 
June 12, 2014, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Orange County. No. 12CVS713. Orlando F. Hudson, Jr., Judge.
King v. Town of Chapel Hill, 743 S.E.2d 666, 2013 N.C. App. LEXIS 619 (N.C. Ct. App., 2013)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED IN PART; REVERSED IN PART; REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-In a case involving the scope of a municipality's power to regulate both the business of towing vehicles parked in private lots and the use of mobile telephones while driving, the Supreme Court held that the Town of Chapel Hill is generally permitted to regulate vehicle towing and that it acted within its authority by enacting signage, notice, and payment requirements for towing from private lots; [2]-However, the Supreme Court also held that Chapel Hill exceeded those powers by imposing a fee schedule and prohibiting towing companies from charging credit card fees; [3]-The Supreme Court also held that the legislature's comprehensive scheme regulating mobile telephone usage on the state's streets and highways precludes municipalities from intruding into this sphere wholly occupied by the state.


OUTCOME: The decision of the appellate court reversing the trial court's issuance of a permanent injunction barring enforcement of both the towing ordinance and the mobile phone ordinance was reversed in part and affirmed in part, and the matter was remanded.


 

19 of 825 DOCUMENTS



Beroth Oil Co. v. N.C. DOT


No. 390PA11-2


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


367 N.C. 333; 757 S.E.2d 466; 2014 N.C. LEXIS 291


September 3, 2013, Heard in the Supreme Court 
April 11, 2014, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Counsel Amended May 23, 2014.


PRIOR HISTORY: Forsyth County. No. 10CVS6926. Lindsay R. Davis, Jr., Judge.
Beroth Oil Co. v. N.C. DOT, 220 N.C. App. 419, 725 S.E.2d 651, 2012 N.C. App. LEXIS 663 (N.C. Ct. App., 2012)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED IN PART, VACATED IN PART, AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether it was error to deny plaintiffs landowners class certification in an inverse condemnation claim against defendant North Carolina Department of Transportation. HOLDINGS: [1]-It was error to analyze the merits of their claim pursuant to an ends-means test because the issue was whether they met the requirements of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1A-1, N.C. R. Civ. P. 23; [2]-It was not error to deny their motion to certify a class because the unique nature of property and the large number of diverse tracts caused individual issues to predominate over common issues of law and fact, as the proposed class included over 800 landowners with different property interests, not all of whom were affected in the same way, liability could be established only after examining the circumstances of each property, under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 136-112 (2011), and the extent of damages was not a collateral issue.


OUTCOME: Judgment vacated in part and affirmed in part.

21 of 825 DOCUMENTS



Hest Techs., Inc. v. State ex rel. Perdue


No. 169A11-2


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


366 N.C. 289; 749 S.E.2d 429; 2012 N.C. LEXIS 1002


October 17, 2012, Heard in the Supreme Court 
December 14, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Hest Techs. v. N.C., 134 S. Ct. 99, 187 L. Ed. 2d 34, 2013 U.S. LEXIS 5319 (U.S., Oct. 7, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY: From NC Court of Appeals. (11-459). From Guildford. (08CVS0457).
Hest Techs., Inc. v. State ex rel. Purdue, 725 S.E.2d 10, 2012 N.C. App. LEXIS 328 (N.C. Ct. App., 2012)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff long-distance telephone and Internet companies filed a motion to modify a preliminary injunction challenging the constitutionality of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-306.4 under the First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I, and N.C. Const. art. I, § 14. The North Carolina Court of Appeals held that the statute was unconstitutionally overbroad. The State of North Carolina appealed.


OVERVIEW: The state's highest court held that while the companies had separated the bet element from the game of chance feature by offering free sweepstakes entries, the North Carolina legislature considered the sweepstakes systems to be the functional equivalent of gambling. Section 14-306.4 primarily regulated non-communicative conduct rather than protected speech. Section 14-306.4(b) (2011) made it unlawful for any person to operate, or place into operation, an electronic machine or device to conduct a sweepstakes through the use of an entertaining display. There was no speaker- or content-based restriction related to the sweepstakes result. The announcement of the winner was not protected speech as it was an incidental part of the non-communicative activity of conducting the sweepstakes. The statute was not overbroad as it concerned only with the attachment of an announcement of a sweepstakes result to the game, which created the functional equivalent of a gambling environment and encouraged the ills the legislature sought to remedy. Even if § 14-306.4 burdened some speech, the First Amendment did not prevent restrictions directed at conduct from imposing incidental burdens on speech.


OUTCOME: The decision of the appellate court was reversed. The case was remanded to the appellate court for further remand proceedings not inconsistent with the opinion.

State v. Heien


No. 380PA11


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


366 N.C. 271; 737 S.E.2d 351; 2012 N.C. LEXIS 1003


May 7, 2012, Heard in the Supreme Court 
December 14, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at, Judgment entered by State v. Heien, 741 S.E.2d 1, 2013 N.C. App. LEXIS 331 (N.C. Ct. App., Apr. 2, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY: From NC Court of Appeals. (11-52). From Surry. (09CRS5141718).
State v. Heien, 714 S.E.2d 827, 2011 N.C. App. LEXIS 1750 (N.C. Ct. App., 2011)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the trial court denied defendant's motion to suppress drugs seized by police during a warrantless search of his vehicle, he was convicted of possession of cocaine and attempting to traffic in cocaine by transportation. He appealed; the North Carolina Court of Appeals vacated his convictions. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: An officer, seeing that the right brake light of a vehicle did not come on when the vehicle slowed, stopped the vehicle. Defendant, its owner and a passenger, consented to a search of the vehicle; the officer found cocaine. The trial court denied defendant's motion to suppress, finding that the officer had a reasonable and articulable suspicion that the vehicle and driver were violating a law requiring properly functioning brake lights and that the seizure was thus constitutionally valid. The intermediate appellate court held that former N.C. Gen. Stat. § 20-129 (2009) required only one brake light; as the driver was not violating the law, the stop was objectively unreasonable and violated defendant's rights under the Fourth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. IV. Assuming without deciding that this interpretation of § 20-129 was correct, the high court held that under the totality of the circumstances, the officer had a reasonable, articulable suspicion that § 20-129 was being violated, and his mistake of law was objectively reasonable. Therefore, the traffic stop did not violate defendant's Fourth Amendment rights, and the trial court properly denied his motion to suppress.


OUTCOME: The high court reversed the decision of the intermediate appellate court and remanded the case to that court for additional proceedings.

 

23 of 825 DOCUMENTS



Lanvale Props., LLC v. County of Cabarrus


No. 438PA10


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


366 N.C. 142; 731 S.E.2d 800; 2012 N.C. LEXIS 644


October 17, 2011, Heard in the Supreme Court 
August 24, 2012, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: NC Court of Appeals. No. 09-1621. Robert C. Hunter, Judge.
Lanvale Props. v. County of Cabarrus, 206 N.C. App. 762, 699 S.E.2d 139, 2010 N.C. App. LEXIS 1719 (2010)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant County adopted an adequate public facilities ordinance (APFO) that effectively conditioned the approval of new residential construction projects on developers paying a fee to subsidize new school construction. Plaintiff developer filed a declaratory judgment action, challenging the validity of the APFO. The trial court held that the APFO was invalid. The Court of Appeals (North Carolina) affirmed. The County appealed.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to the APFO, the County's Planning and Zoning Commission reviewed all proposed residential developments, except those located within two territorial jurisdictions, to determine if the new homes would exacerbate overcrowding in the County's two public schools systems. The Commission denied preliminary approval applications for a number of proposed developments based upon insufficient public school capacity. However, the County Board of Commissioners ultimately approved the developments on appeal once developers executed consent agreements designed to mitigate the impact of their developments on public school capacity. The County lacked the statutory authority to adopt the APFO pursuant to its general zoning powers under N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 153A-4, 153A-340, and 153A-341 because the APFO was not a zoning ordinance. Rather, it impermissibly placed the burden of funding public school construction on developers by using a revenue generating mechanism that was disguised as a zoning ordinance. Furthermore, 2004 N.C. Sess. Laws 39, which was intended to address APFO enforcement concerns involving municipalities located within the County, did not authorize the enactment of the APFO.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.


 

24 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Yencer


No. 365PA10


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


365 N.C. 292; 718 S.E.2d 615; 2011 N.C. LEXIS 902


March 15, 2011, Heard in the Supreme Court 
November 10, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Mecklenburg County. Nos. 09-1; 06CRS200690. James A. Wynn Jr., Judge.


DISPOSITION:  [***1] REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with driving while impaired and reckless driving. She filed a motion to suppress, contending that the Campus Police Act, N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 74G-1 to -13 (2009), violated the Establishment Clause of the First Amendment. The trial court denied the motion, and defendant pleaded guilty, reserving her right to appeal. The North Carolina Court of Appeals reversed the denial of the motion to suppress, and the State appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged by a campus police officer after she was stopped driving near a college campus. The Court of Appeals held that the Campus Police Act granted an unconstitutional delegation of discretionary power to a religious institution. The court considered defendant's Establishment Clause challenge to the Act using the three-part test of Lemon v. Kurtzman and concluded that the Act had a secular legislative purpose, as stated in N.C. Gen. Stat. § 74G-2(a). The college, a private liberal arts college affiliated with the Presbyterian Church (PC), was not a church and its primary purpose of secular education was not religious in nature. The potential influence of the PC over the college was minimal, as the church did not run or control the college and had no role in management or policy decisions. Because the campus police agency benefited the college's secular rather than religious activities, the statute did not give rise to excessive entanglement or have the primary effect of advancing or inhibiting religion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the Court of Appeals.

 

25 of 825 DOCUMENTS



Libertarian Party v. State


No. 479A09


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


365 N.C. 41; 707 S.E.2d 199; 2011 N.C. LEXIS 142


September 9, 2010, Heard in the Supreme Court 
March 11, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Libertarian Party of N.C. v. State, 200 N.C. App. 323, 688 S.E.2d 700, 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1681 (N.C. Ct. App., 2009)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] MODIFIED AND AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, political parties and their candidates, sued appellees, the Attorney General, the North Carolina Board of Elections, and its executive director, alleging N.C. Gen. Stat. § 163-96(a)(2)'s (2009) ballot access requirements violated N.C. Const. art. I, §§ 12, 14, and 19. The trial court upheld the statute, and the Court of Appeals (North Carolina) affirmed. Appellants sought further review pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-30(2).


OVERVIEW: N.C. Gen. Stat. § 163-96(a)(2) (2009) made the parties get two percent of the votes for governor in the last election or the signatures of that number of voters. The supreme court held these requirements were not subject to strict scrutiny because they did not severely burden associational rights, as (1) minority parties seeking recognition had over three and one-half years to get the required signatures, (2) the statute put few restrictions on signatories, since they did not have to register with or promise to vote for candidates of the party seeking recognition, and could vote in a major party's primary, (3) a few supporters could get the required signatures, and (4) the requirements were readily achievable. They did not violate due process, free speech and assembly, and equal protection provisions in N.C. Const. art. I, §§ 12, 14, and 19 because they imposed a reasonable hurdle to ballot access, as (1) signatories were not disqualified for voting in another party's primary, (2) they were more permissive than requirements upheld by the U.S. Supreme Court, and (3) they did not discriminate against minor parties or operate to freeze the political status quo of a two-party system.


OUTCOME: The opinion of the Court of Appeals was modified insofar as it incorrectly held the ballot access requirements were subject to strict scrutiny, and, as modified, that opinion was affirmed.

State v. Lane

Motion granted by State v. Lane, 792 S.E.2d 798, 2016 N.C. LEXIS 785 (N.C., 2016)
Motion granted by State v. Lane, 2016 N.C. LEXIS 897 (N.C., Sept. 30, 2016)

Motion granted by State v. Lane, 2017 N.C. LEXIS 12 (N.C., Jan. 12, 2017)

DISPOSITION:  [***1] NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder in the Superior Court for Wayne County (North Carolina) and sentenced to death. Defendant appealed as of right to the supreme court. The supreme court remanded the case for further hearings, findings of fact, and conclusions of law. Following entry of an order on remand, the supreme court allowed defendant's motion for supplemental briefing following the remand.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred by granting his motion to discharge appointed counsel and proceed pro se because the undisputed facts showed that, as articulated in Indiana v. Edwards, he came within the category of "gray area" or "borderline competent" defendants who were competent to stand trial but nonetheless lacked the capacity to conduct trial proceedings without the assistance of counsel. The supreme court disagreed. Defendant was able to respond to the trial court's inquiries in a manner that indicated that he: (1) understood the charges and proceedings against him and the range of possible punishments; (2) had been clearly advised of his right to counsel; and (3) appreciated the consequences of his decision to waive that right. Defendant's technical legal knowledge was not relevant to an assessment of his knowing exercise of the right to defend himself. Defendant was well aware of his limitations, and the record established that he knew what he was doing and his choice was made with eyes open.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


 

27 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Whitaker


No. 21A10


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


364 N.C. 404; 700 S.E.2d 215; 2010 N.C. LEXIS 737; 63 A.L.R.6th 755


September 7, 2010, Heard in the Supreme Court 
October 8, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Whitaker, 689 S.E.2d 395, 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 2198 (N.C. Ct. App., 2009)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was found guilty by a jury of eleven counts of possession of a firearm by a felon in violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-415.1. The trial court entered judgment on one count, sentencing defendant to 18 to 22 months of imprisonment. Defendant appealed to the North Carolina Court of Appeals, which in a divided decision found no error in defendant's conviction upon which he was sentenced. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the 2004 amendment to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-415.1 was an unconstitutional ex post facto law or alternatively, a bill of attainder. The court held that the trial court's judgment against defendant was not for any prior act but was consistent with defendant's possession of a firearm in 2006, over two years after § 14-415.1 was amended to prohibit such action. In the strictest sense, defendant's conviction was for an offense that he committed after his actions were deemed criminal, namely the possession of any firearm by a felon. The court found that the statute was rationally related to the legislature's nonpunitive intent to protect the public from future violent actions of those it had deemed to have a heightened disregard of the law. The scope of the 2004 amendment was not excessive when compared with the purpose of protecting public safety. Because the prohibition of possession of firearms by felons did not operate as punishment on a selected group of persons without a judicial trial, § 14-415.1 was not a bill of attainder.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the Court of Appeals.

 

28 of 825 DOCUMENTS



State v. Mumford


No. 32PA10


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


364 N.C. 394; 699 S.E.2d 911; 2010 N.C. LEXIS 730


September 9, 2010, Heard in the Supreme Court 
October 8, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at State v. Mumford, 2011 N.C. App. LEXIS 1902 (N.C. Ct. App., Sept. 6, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Mumford, 688 S.E.2d 458, 2010 N.C. App. LEXIS 35 (N.C. Ct. App., 2010)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted on five counts of felony serious injury by vehicle, in violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 20-141.4(a3) (2009), and one count of misdemeanor hit and run, but was found not guilty of driving while impaired, in violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 20-138.1. The Court of Appeals (North Carolina) vacated the judgments. The State sought discretionary review pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-31.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of the greater offense of felony serious injury by vehicle but acquitted of the lesser offense of driving while impaired. While the verdicts were certainly inconsistent, they were not mutually exclusive. Because the case presented nothing more than mere inconsistency, defendant was not entitled to relief. N.C. Gen. Stat. § 20-141.4(a3) did not require a conviction of driving while impaired under N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 20-138.1 or -138.2, but only required a finding that the defendant was engaged in the conduct described under either of these offenses. There was sufficient evidence presented at trial to support defendant's convictions for felony serious injury by vehicle under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 20-141.4(a3). Accordingly, the Court of Appeals erred in vacating the convictions. Further, the Court of Appeals erred in vacating the portion of the judgment ordering defendant to pay restitution. While the mere presentation of a worksheet was not sufficient to support the award of restitution, any error in the determination of restitution was not prejudicial to defendant because defendant would pay the lesser of the actual amount owed or the amount ordered.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the Court of Appeals and remanded the case to the Court of Appeals for consideration of assignments of error not addressed in that court's initial opinion.

State v. Bowditch


No. 448PA09


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


364 N.C. 335; 700 S.E.2d 1; 2010 N.C. LEXIS 741


May 10, 2010, Heard in the Supreme Court 
October 8, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Stay denied by State v. Bowditch, 703 S.E.2d 151, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 825 (N.C., Oct. 12, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Bowditch, Plemmons & Waters, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 866 (N.C., Oct. 7, 2010)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In three consolidated cases, defendants received notice that the State intended to enforce satellite-based monitoring (SBM) pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 14-208.40 to -208.45 (2009). The Superior Court, Buncombe County (North Carolina), allowed defendants' motions to dismiss on constitutional grounds and dismissed the State's petitions. The State gave notice of appeal, and defendants obtained discretionary review.


OVERVIEW: Defendants each pleaded guilty to multiple counts of taking indecent liberties with a child. All of the offenses occurred before the SBM statutes took effect. Defendants disputed their eligibility for SBM, arguing that their participation would have violated guarantees against ex post fact laws contained in U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, cl. 1 and N.C. Const. art. I, § 16. However, the SBM program was enacted with the intent to create a civil, regulatory scheme to protect citizens of North Carolina from the threat posed by the recidivist tendencies of convicted sex offenders. In examining the relevant Mendoza-Martinez factors in detail, the appellate court concluded that neither the purpose nor effect of the SBM program negated the legislature's civil intent. Accordingly, the SBM program was not intended to be criminal punishment and was not punitive in purpose or effect, and subjecting defendants to the SBM program did not violate the Ex Post Facto Clauses of the state or federal constitution.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court and remanded for further proceedings consistent with its opinion.

King v. Beaufort County Bd. of Educ.


No. 480A09


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


364 N.C. 368; 704 S.E.2d 259; 2010 N.C. LEXIS 733


March 22, 2010, Heard in the Supreme Court 
October 8, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: King v. Beaufort County Bd. of Educ., 683 S.E.2d 767, 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1640 (N.C. Ct. App., 2009)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff student filed a complaint alleging that defendants, school officials, violated her state constitutional right to a sound basic education by suspending her without providing for alternative education. The trial court dismissed the complaint pursuant to N.C. R. Civ. P. 12(b)(6) and 12(b)(7). The Court of Appeals, in a divided opinion, affirmed the trial court's ruling in favor of the officials. The student appealed.


OVERVIEW: The student, a high school sophomore, participated in a fight involving numerous students in January of the school year. The school officials suspended the student for the remainder of the school year without offering her alternative education. The court determined that an intermediate scrutiny, rather than strict scrutiny or the rational basis test, should apply to disciplinary decisions involving denial of alternative education to a student on long-term suspension because exclusion from alternative education potentially infringed on the student's state constitutional right to equal educational access. Under the intermediate scrutiny, school administrators were required to articulate a reason when they excluded a long-term suspended student from alternative education. Because the officials were unaware of this new holding when they disciplined the student, on remand, they should be afforded the opportunity to explain why they denied access to alternative education.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the Court of Appeals and remanded the case for further remand to the trial court to allow the officials the opportunity to explain their decision.

Jones v. Keller


No. 518PA09


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


364 N.C. 249; 698 S.E.2d 49; 2010 N.C. LEXIS 587


February 16, 2010, Heard in the Supreme Court 
August 27, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition denied by Jones v. Keller, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 3201 (U.S., Apr. 25, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY: Jones v. Keller, 700 S.E.2d 922, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 668 (N.C., 2010)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court, Wayne County (North Carolina) allowed petitioner's application for writ of habeas corpus and ordered his unconditional release from prison. The Department of Corrections (the DOC)'s motion for a temporary stay and its petition for writ of certiorari, pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-32(b), were granted.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted of murder and was sentenced to death. The appellate court later vacated the death sentence and, on remand, petitioner was sentenced to a term of life imprisonment. Over 30 years later, the trial court concluded that petitioner was entitled to be awarded good time, gain time, and merit time by the DOC, including calculation of his date of unconditional release. Further, the trial court found that petitioner had served the entirety of the sentence imposed and was entitled to release. However, the DOC's interpretation that petitioner's good time, gain time, and merit time credits were not awarded to him for purposes of unconditional release was statutorily and constitutionally permissible. The fact that petitioner was serving a sentence for first-degree murder reasonably suggested that he presented a greater threat to society than other prisoners. Thus, the DOC had a rational basis for denying petitioner good time, gain time, and merit time for the purposes of unconditional release, even though these same credits had been awarded for that purpose to other prisoners with determinate sentences convicted under statutes other than N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-2.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment.


 

32 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Ward


No. 68A99-3


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


364 N.C. 157; 694 S.E.2d 729; 2010 N.C. LEXIS 418


January 6, 2010, Heard in the Supreme Court 
June 17, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Ward, 354 N.C. 231, 555 S.E.2d 251, 2001 N.C. LEXIS 1097 (2001)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED IN PART; CERTIORARI IMPROVIDENTLY ALLOWED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of, inter alia, felony murder, conspiracy to commit murder, robbery with a dangerous weapon, and felonious breaking or entering, larceny, and conspiracy to commit breaking or entering and larceny. The case was remanded for resentencing. The Superior Court, Halifax County (North Carolina) denied defendant's motion to bifurcate the sentencing proceeding. The supreme court granted defendant's petition for writ of certiorari.


OVERVIEW: Because N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2005(e) (2009) did not explicitly prohibit a trial court from submitting the special issue of mental retardation to the jury in a bifurcated, rather than unitary, capital sentencing proceeding, the supreme court held that such a determination was left to the sound discretion of the trial judges. The plain language of § 15A-2005 indicated that the jury would make two separate determinations, at two distinct points during the sentencing proceeding: first, on the special issue of mental retardation, and next, only if defendant was found not to be mentally retarded, the sentence to be imposed. By mandating that a jury first consider and answer the special issue on mental retardation, the supreme court concluded that § 15A-2005(c) does not preclude a bifurcated proceeding, but rather contemplated that only after completing and returning a "not mentally retarded "verdict on the first issue could a jury consider evidence of aggravating and mitigating factors. Before denying defendant's motion, the trial judge hear extensive argument from both sides. Nothing in the record indicated that trial court's decision was arbitrary or unsupported by reason.


OUTCOME: The order denying defendant's motion to bifurcate was affirmed.

 

33 of 825 DOCUMENTS



Baxter v. Danny Nicholson, Inc.


No. 351PA08


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


363 N.C. 829; 690 S.E.2d 265; 2010 N.C. LEXIS 202


November 18, 2009, Heard in the Supreme Court 
March 12, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at, Judgment entered by Baxter v. Danny Nicholson, Inc., 2010 N.C. App. LEXIS 1829 (N.C. Ct. App., Sept. 21, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY: Baxter v. Danny Nicholson, Inc., 191 N.C. App. 168, 661 S.E.2d 892, 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 1149 (2008)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The industrial commission awarded plaintiff employee workers' compensation. Defendant employer appealed. The Court of Appeals (North Carolina) vacated the award on the grounds that the commissioner was without authority because his successor had been appointed. The employee appealed, arguing that the commissioner was authorized under N.C. Const. art. VI, § 10 and N.C. Gen. Stat. § 128-7 because the successor had not yet taken the oath of office.


OVERVIEW: The commissioner, an appointed public officer, was notified that his service as a commissioner was at an end and that his successor had been appointed. After receiving the notification letter, but before the successor commissioner had taken the oath of office, the commissioner sat on a three-person panel, heard the employee's case, and awarded the benefits. Under N.C. Const. art. VI, § 10, the commissioner held his position until his successor was chosen and qualified. Moreover, under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 128-7, the commissioner was required to continue in his office until his successor was elected or appointed, and duly qualified. Pursuant to N.C. Const. art. VI, § 7 and N.C. Gen. Stat. § 11-7, the newly appointed successor was required to take the oath of office before he was qualified to assume the commissioner's duties. The General Assembly expanded on the constitutional provisions to require that a public servant swear an oath before taking office, and to ensure that the office was not vacated during any delay between the appointment of a successor and his lawful entry into office upon becoming qualified by taking the oath.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeals' opinion and remanded the matter to that court for consideration of the substantive issues raised in the employer's appeal.


 

34 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Bunch


No. 203A09


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


363 N.C. 841; 689 S.E.2d 866; 2010 N.C. LEXIS 201


September 10, 2009, Heard in the Supreme Court 
March 12, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended March 17, 2010.


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Bunch, 675 S.E.2d 103, 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 416 (N.C. Ct. App., 2009)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of robbery with a dangerous weapon and first-degree murder under the felony murder rule, a violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-17. The trial court sentenced defendant to life imprisonment without parole for the felony murder and to a consecutive term of 103 to 133 months for the robbery. The Court of Appeals (North Carolina) affirmed. Defendant appealed, raising a violation of N.C. Const. art. I, § 24.


OVERVIEW: The trial court informed jurors of two elements of felony murder and instructed them on the underlying felonies of burglary and robbery with a dangerous weapon. In its jury charge, the trial court only recited the first portion of the part of the North Carolina Pattern Jury Instruction that explained what the State had to prove beyond a reasonable doubt to obtain a conviction of felony murder. The trial court did not explicitly include a reference to the effect that defendant was the killer, or that defendant's acts were the proximate cause of the victim's death. While use of the pattern instructions was encouraged, it was not required. The trial court's instruction mirrored the definition of felony murder. The error was not structural, and the harmless error standard was applied. In light of the overwhelming evidence that defendant caused the victim's death, even if the trial court's instructions were erroneous, any error was harmless. Even if the jurors had received the complete pattern instruction for felony murder, there was no reasonable probability that outcome would have been different.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

35 of 825 DOCUMENTS



Copper v. Denlinger


No. 526A08


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


363 N.C. 784; 688 S.E.2d 426; 2010 N.C. LEXIS 33


May 5, 2009, Heard in the Supreme Court 
January 29, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Copper v. Denlinger, 193 N.C. App. 249, 667 S.E.2d 470, 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 1817 (2008)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] REVERSED IN PART; DISCRETIONARY REVIEW IMPROVIDENTLY ALLOWED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a county school board and superintendent, sought review of a decision from the Court of Appeals (North Carolina), which reversed the dismissal of plaintiffs, students and parents, state constitutional claim against the board, the 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claim against the superintendent in her individual capacity, for alleged violations of his procedural due process rights, and the dismissal of the claim concerning the board's gang policy.


OVERVIEW: The students and parents alleged that the board and superintendent subjected minority students to more severe disciplinary measures for less serious offenses than white students including suspensions without due process of law and labeling minority students as gang affiliated. With regard to the state constitutional claims against the board, the court found that under N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 115C-45(c) and 391(e), the students always had the statutory right to appeal; thus, the complaint's allegation that the students were never given that opportunity failed. Thus, an adequate remedy existed at state law to redress the alleged injury, and the direct constitutional claim was barred. As to the § 1983 claim against the superintendent, the court held that the students could show no claim under § 1983 for violation of procedural due process when their own allegations revealed that the students and the parents failed to avail themselves of the due process offered under state law. The allegations in the complaint did not demonstrate how a meeting between a student with his mother and school representatives violated the students' right to procedural due process.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appeals court's decision and affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the single student's claims, and left undisturbed the appeals court's decision to reversed and remand for additional proceedings as to the issue of the gang policy, despite finding that discretionary review was improvidently allowed.


 

36 of 825 DOCUMENTS



State v. Williams


No. 506A07


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


363 N.C. 689; 686 S.E.2d 493; 2009 N.C. LEXIS 1292


September 8, 2009, Heard in the Supreme Court 
December 11, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected December 17, 2009.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Williams v. North Carolina, 131 S. Ct. 149, 178 L. Ed. 2d 90, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 6797 (U.S., 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Williams, 676 S.E.2d 475, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 580 (N.C., 2009)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of two counts of first-degree murder. Following the convictions, the trial court declared a mistrial as to the penalty proceedings. New counsel was appointed and a different jury returned a binding recommendation that defendant be sentenced to death. Appeal was taken as of right pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a) from the judgments imposing a death sentence entered in Superior Court Cumberland County (North Carolina).


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred in not removing an attorney as his counsel or by not holding a hearing to determine whether there was a conflict of interest between defendant and the attorney. In the absence of a request for the appointment of substitute counsel, the trial court was not required to hold any hearing. Any potential conflict that existed between defendant and the attorney would not have been apparent to the trial court, such as to compel the trial court to ex mero motu conduct a hearing on the matter. Defendant was not entitled to file pro se motions while represented by counsel and the trial court did not err in refusing to rule on defendant's pro se speedy trial motion. The trial court did not lack jurisdiction to enter a judgment sentencing defendant to death. While N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(a) did envision the same trial judge presiding over a defendant's guilt phase and penalty proceeding, the statute also envisioned the proceeding being held immediately or soon after the defendant was found guilty of a capital offense. Such a scenario was impossible in the instant case because of defendant's unprovoked attack on one of his attorneys.


OUTCOME: There was no error in defendant's convictions or sentences.


 

37 of 825 DOCUMENTS


Beaufort County Bd. of Educ. v. Beaufort County Bd. of Comm'rs


No. 106PA08


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


363 N.C. 500; 681 S.E.2d 278; 2009 N.C. LEXIS 739


September 9, 2008, Heard in the Supreme Court 
August 28, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by, Motion granted by Beaufort County Bd. of Educ. v. Beaufort County Bd. of Comm'rs, 363 N.C. 663, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 1454 (2009)
Motion denied by Beaufort BOE v. Beaufort Bd. of Comm., 684 S.E.2d 691, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 905 (N.C., 2009)
Motion granted by Beaufort Bd. of Educ. v. Beaufort Bd. of Comm., 684 S.E.2d 691, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 911 (N.C., 2009)
Rehearing denied by Beaufort County Bd. of Educ. v. Beaufort County Bd. of Comm'rs, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 1525 (N.C., Oct. 8, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: Beaufort County Bd. of Educ. v. Beaufort County Bd. of Comm'rs, 188 N.C. App. 399, 656 S.E.2d 296, 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 230 (2008)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff school board brought suit against defendant county commission pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 115C-431(c) (2007), seeking funding for school operations. A jury found that the school board needed $ 10,200,000, and the trial court entered a judgment requiring the commission to appropriate that amount to the school board. The commission appealed, and the North Carolina Court of Appeals affirmed. The commission again appealed.


OVERVIEW: The commission alleged that by allowing the court system to decide the level of funding for public education, § 115C-431(c) impermissibly delegated the legislature's constitutional duty under N.C. Const. art. IX, § 2(1), to provide for a general and uniform system of free public schools. The court upheld the constitutionality of § 115C-431(c), finding that the legislature had merely delegated to the courts the fact-finding duty of determining the cost of the scheme exhaustively defined by the legislature, which was a proper role of the judiciary. However, the trial court erred in instructing the jury on the definition of "needed" in § 115C-431(c). A restrictive definition was necessary to preserve the discretionary authority of county commissions under article IX, § 2(2). The jury could consider the educational policies of the state, the budgetary request of the local board, the financial resources of the county, and the fiscal policies of the commissioners, pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 115C-426(e) (2007). Anything beyond this measure of damages impermissibly infringed upon the discretionary authority of the county commission and could not be awarded by a jury.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the Court of Appeals and remanded the case to that court for further remand to the trial court for a new trial.

 

38 of 825 DOCUMENTS


Blankenship v. Bartlett


No. 455PA06-2


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


363 N.C. 518; 681 S.E.2d 759; 2009 N.C. LEXIS 743


February 23, 2009, Heard in the Supreme Court 
August 28, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Blankenship v. Bartlett, 184 N.C. App. 327, 646 S.E.2d 584, 2007 N.C. App. LEXIS 1452 (2007)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, attorneys qualified for superior court judge and a voter, challenged an order of the Court of Appeals (North Carolina), which reversed a judgment entered in their favor in their suit against defendants, North Carolina State Board of Elections and the attorney general, alleging that the 1993 amendment to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-41 unconstitutionally created an additional superior court judgeship in Wake County Superior Court District 10A.


OVERVIEW: Voters in the county were divided into four districts for purposes of electing superior court judges. The General Assembly gave residents in District 10C one-fifth, or 20 percent, of the voting power of residents in District 10A. Residents of Districts 10B and 10D had one-fourth, or 25 percent, of the voting power of residents in District 10A. The supreme court held that the Equal Protection Clause, N.C. Const. art. I, § 19, applied to the creation of an additional judgeship in District 10A. The right to vote in superior court elections on substantially equal terms was a quasi-fundamental right subject to a heightened level of scrutiny. Plaintiffs demonstrated gross disparity in voting power between similarly situated residents. Residents of District 10A had a voting power roughly five times greater than residents of District 10C, four and a half times greater than residents of District 10B, and four times greater than residents of District 10D. The trial court did not err in admitting an exhibit attached to an affidavit. It admitted the evidence in its discretion under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 8C-1, N.C. R. Evid. 803(8) (2007) and made findings of fact based on trustworthy information.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the judgment and remanded the cause to the court of appeals for further remand to the trial court with orders to hold a new hearing and determine whether the State could meet its burden of demonstrating significant interests that justified the legislature's subdivisions within District 10 and to show that the disparity in voter strength was not substantially greater than necessary to accommodate those interests.

 

39 of 825 DOCUMENTS


Britt v. State


No. 488A07


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


363 N.C. 546; 681 S.E.2d 320; 2009 N.C. LEXIS 815


May 5, 2008, Heard in the Supreme Court 
August 28, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Britt v. State, 185 N.C. App. 610, 649 S.E.2d 402, 2007 N.C. App. LEXIS 1938 (2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff gun owner challenged a judgment of the Court of Appeals (North Carolina), which affirmed an order granting defendant State summary judgment in the owner's action alleging that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-415.1, as amended in 2004, violated his rights under N.C. Const. art. I, § 30.


OVERVIEW: The owner pleaded guilty to felony possession with intent to sell and deliver a controlled substance in 1979. His civil rights were fully restored in 1987. In 2004, he General Assembly amended § 14-415.1 to prohibit the possession of all firearms by an person convicted of any felon. The supreme court held that the application of § 14-415.1, as amended in 2004, to the owner violated N.C. Const. art. I, § 30. Pursuant to § 14-415.1, the State unreasonably divested the owner of his right to own a firearm, and such action violated his right to keep and bear arms. The State did not argue that any aspect of the owner's crime involved violence or the threat of violence. The owner completed his sentence without incident in 1982. His right to possess firearms was restored in 1987. No evidence was presented indicating that he was dangerous or had ever misused firearms. The owner affirmatively demonstrated that he was not among the class of citizens who posed a threat to public peace and safety. As applied to the owner, the 2004 version of § 14-451.1 was an unreasonable regulation, not fairly related to the preservation of public peace and safety.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the decision of the court of appeals and remanded the case to that court for remand to the superior court for further proceedings.

 

40 of 825 DOCUMENTS



State v. Locklear


No. 578A05


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


363 N.C. 438; 681 S.E.2d 293; 2009 N.C. LEXIS 814


September 8, 2008, Heard in the Supreme Court 
August 28, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion denied by State v. Locklear, 363 N.C. 660, 684 S.E.2d 439, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 872 (N.C., Sept. 9, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Locklear, 2008 N.C. LEXIS 462 (N.C., May 8, 2008)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] NO ERROR IN GUILT-INNOCENCE PHASE; DEATH SENTENCE VACATED; REMANDED FOR NEW CAPITAL SENTENCING PROCEEDING.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed as of right from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered in Superior Court, Robeson County, North Carolina, upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder. Following a mental retardation hearing, the jury found defendant was not mentally retarded. Defendant appealed his capital conviction to the court, which allowed his motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to his other convictions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred in allowing the State to introduce evidence that defendant killed another woman in 1997. The court found no error in the trial court's ruling that the evidence of the first death was admissible for purposes of showing defendant's knowledge, plan, opportunity, intent, modus operandi, and motive to kill the second woman. The court also rejected defendant's arguments as to the admissibility of other evidence and ineffective assistance of counsel. At sentencing, defense counsel unsuccessfully requested an instruction that, should the jurors find defendant mentally retarded, he would be sentenced to life imprisonment without parole. Remanding for sentencing, the court noted that defendant presented substantial evidence of mental retardation to the jury during the sentencing proceeding. The court determined that the requested instruction was proper under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2005 (2007). Pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-1443(a) (2007), the court further concluded that the error prejudiced defendant, noting that the jury found as mitigating circumstances many facts that would also tend to establish mental retardation on the part of defendant.


OUTCOME: The court found no error in defendant's trial, but vacate his death sentence and remanded the case for a new capital sentencing proceeding.


 

41 of 825 DOCUMENTS



State v. Maness


No. 402A06


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


363 N.C. 261; 677 S.E.2d 796; 2009 N.C. LEXIS 621


September 10, 2008, Heard in the Supreme Court 
June 18, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended July 20, 2009.
Petition denied by Maness v. North Carolina, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 3027 (U.S., Apr. 5, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Maness, 668 S.E.2d 568, 2008 N.C. LEXIS 809 (N.C., 2008)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, as of right pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a), appealed the judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by the Superior Court, Brunswick County (North Carolina), following a jury verdict finding him guilty of first-degree murder. Defendant had murdered a police officer during the performance of his duties.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant was found to have failed to show any trial error with regard to the jury selection challenges raised. The court found that the trial court did not commit prejudicial error when it denied a jury request to review certain exhibits as they were admitted solely for the purpose of illustrating an expert's testimony and the jury already had seen the exhibits. The court further found that there was some inadmissible material in the exhibits. The court further held that the trial court did not err by denying defendant's motion for a mistrial, made when law enforcement officers approached the jury box to purportedly block the victim's mother from seeing autopsy photographs, as the trial court acted promptly and effectively to regain control of the courtroom and the court refused to second-guess the trial court to presume that the incident was fatally prejudicial. The court further found no error with regard to the trial court denying defendant's motion to dismiss the charge of robbery with a dangerous weapon, and the court found that the sentence of death was not disproportionate, excessive or influenced by passion, prejudice, or any other arbitrary factor.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the sentence of death.


 

42 of 825 DOCUMENTS



N.C. Dep't of Corr. v. N.C. Med. Bd.


No. 51PA08


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


363 N.C. 189; 675 S.E.2d 641; 2009 N.C. LEXIS 349


November 18, 2008, Heard in the Supreme Court 
May 1, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Dep't of Corr. v. Med. Bd., 672 S.E.2d 20, 2008 N.C. LEXIS 991 (N.C., 2008)
Brown v. Beck, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 60084 (E.D.N.C., Apr. 7, 2006)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, the North Carolina Department of Correction, its secretary, and a warden, sought an injunction prohibiting defendant North Caroline Medial Board from disciplining physicians for participating in an execution and a declaratory judgment delineating the parties' rights with regards to executions. The Medical Board challenged the ruling of the Superior Court, Wake County (North Carolina) that executions were not medical procedures.


OVERVIEW: The medical board issued a position statement, which prohibited physicians from participating in executions. Therefore, physicians declined to participate in executions in any manner, which resulted in a de facto moratorium on executions in North Carolina. The supreme court held that plaintiffs had standing to litigate the issue because they could not carry out their statutory duty to execute condemned inmates under the Execution Protocol without subjecting a physician to discipline by the Medical Board. There was a genuine controversy between plaintiffs and the Medical Board arising out of conflicting contentions as to their respective legal rights and liabilities under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15-190 (2007). Section 15-190, by its plain language, envisioned physician participation in executions in some professional capacity. The Medical Board's position statement exceeded its authority under Chapter 90 because the statement directly contravened the specific requirement of physician presence found in § 15-190. The language of the Protocol did not overstep the statutory authority of the Governor and Council of State to determine and approve the exact means of execution.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the trial court's order.


 

43 of 825 DOCUMENTS



State v. Garcell


No. 465A06


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


363 N.C. 10; 678 S.E.2d 618; 2009 N.C. LEXIS 239


May 5, 2008, Heard in the Supreme Court 
March 20, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition denied by Garcell v. North Carolina, 558 U.S. 999, 130 S. Ct. 510, 175 L. Ed. 2d 362, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 7811 (2009)
Writ dismissed by State v. Garcell, 367 N.C. 328, 755 S.E.2d 53, 2014 N.C. LEXIS 181 (2014)
Motion dismissed by, As moot State v. Garcell, 2016 N.C. LEXIS 323 (N.C., Apr. 13, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Garcell, 660 S.E.2d 894, 2008 N.C. LEXIS 248 (N.C., 2008)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] NO ERROR; MOTION FOR APPROPRIATE RELIEF DENIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury found defendant guilty of first-degree murder, robbery with a dangerous weapon, and conspiracy to commit robbery with a dangerous weapon. The Superior Court, Rutherford County (North Carolina) sentenced defendant to death for the murder and consecutive terms of 77 to 102 months for robbery and 29 to 44 months for conspiracy. Pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a), defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court found no error in defendant's convictions and sentences. Among other things, the supreme court held that the exclusion of jurors who raised questions about their ability to follow the law on the death penalty was proper. A letter from defendant to his mother was properly admitted, as it constituted defendant's admission to the crime in his own words, was not needlessly cumulative, and was not unfairly prejudicial. Because defendant only raised a specific concern regarding the ability of codefendants to hear one another's testimony, it was not error to sequester only codefendants and not other witnesses. With regard to the penalty phase, the supreme court concluded, inter alia, that any error in submitting kidnapping charges to the jury was harmless in light of the robbery conviction also submitted, and because there was no reasonable possibility the sentencing recommendation would have differed since the jury also found the N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(e)(5) and (9) aggravators. Finally, the court concluded that the death sentence was not excessive or disproportionate, noting that defendant violated the victim's home and committed a brutal murder by strangulation.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.
