State v. Taylor


No. 719A05


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


362 N.C. 514; 669 S.E.2d 239; 2008 N.C. LEXIS 986


February 12, 2008, Heard in the Supreme Court 
December 12, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Taylor v. North Carolina, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 5323 (U.S., Oct. 5, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Taylor, 671 S.E.2d 528, 2008 N.C. LEXIS 994 (N.C., 2008)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed an appeal as of right pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered in the Superior Court, Harnett County (North Carolina), on a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder based on premeditation and deliberation and under the felony murder rule. The court allowed defendant's motion to bypass the court of appeals as to his appeal of additional judgments.


OVERVIEW: N.C. Const. art. I, §§ 19 and 23, and federal due process rights were not violated; a failure to follow procedures in securing physical evidence was unintentional. The State's peremptory challenges to three African-American prospective jurors did not establish a pattern of discrimination. There was uncontroverted evidence from which the jury could infer that defendant formed the intent for first-degree murder. His admission that he fired three times in the victim's direction supported the premeditation and deliberation findings. The unanimity requirement was satisfied, even though alternate liability theories were submitted; defendant committed one of the N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-17 felonies. Because the conviction was based on premeditation and deliberation and under the felony murder rule, neither a robbery with a dangerous weapon of the store owners or of a customer merged with the murder conviction. Any prosecutorial misstatement was cured by a jury instruction on its duty as to Issue Three of the Capital Sentencing form. Considering the crime and defendant, as required by N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(d)(2), the case was similar to those in which the death sentence was proportionate.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment and sentence of death were upheld.


 

45 of 825 DOCUMENTS


Andrews v. Haygood


No. 57A07-2


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


362 N.C. 599; 669 S.E.2d 310; 2008 N.C. LEXIS 984


October 13, 2008, Heard in the Supreme Court 
December 12, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Brown v. Nc Dep't of H&Hs, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 4519 (U.S., June 15, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: Andrews v. Haygood, 188 N.C. App. 244, 655 S.E.2d 440, 2008 N.C. App. LEXIS 91 (2008)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED; DISCRETIONARY REVIEW IMPROVIDENTLY ALLOWED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff child sued defendant health care providers for medical malpractice and recovery for injuries sustained at birth. The parties entered into confidential settlement agreements. The trial court determined that appellee, the North Carolina Division of Medical Assistance (DMA) had subrogation rights to the entire amount of the settlement. Appellant trustee appealed. The Court of Appeals of North Carolina affirmed. The trustee appealed.


OVERVIEW: The parties entered into confidential settlement agreements and established a settlement account for the proceeds. Because the child was a North Carolina Medicaid recipient, the DMA sought to recover from the account the amount it paid for her medical expenses. The lower courts determined that the DMA had subrogation rights to the entire amount of the settlement, limited by the statutory provision of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 108A-57(a) (2005) that only one-third of a recovery was subject to subrogation. Because the amount expended by the DMA was less than one-third of the settlement, the courts ordered full reimbursement. The case thus presented the issue of whether the statutory framework governing the State of North Carolina's subrogation claim for medical expenses on a Medicaid recipient's tort claim settlement complied with federal Medicaid law under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1396a(a)(25) (2000). The state supreme court found that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 108A-57(a) was a reasonable framework that comported with the requirements of federal Medicaid law. Therefore, the lower courts did not err in subrogating the child's settlement proceeds to the DMA, subject to the one-third statutory limitation.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was affirmed.

 

46 of 825 DOCUMENTS



State v. Styles


No. 442A07


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


362 N.C. 412; 665 S.E.2d 438; 2008 N.C. LEXIS 685


December 10, 2007, Heard in the Supreme Court 
August 27, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Styles, 185 N.C. App. 271, 648 S.E.2d 214, 2007 N.C. App. LEXIS 1696 (2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed after the Court of Appeals (North Carolina) affirmed a trial court judgment that denied defendant's motion to suppress all evidence obtained as a result of a traffic stop.


OVERVIEW: The issue was whether Fourth Amendment rights were violated by the traffic stop. Reasonable suspicion was the standard for traffic stops, regardless of whether a traffic violation was readily observed or merely suspected. To the extent language in State v. Ivey, 360 N.C. 562, 633 S.E.2d 459 (2006), might be interpreted as requiring probable cause, the supreme court specifically disavowed that interpretation. Defendant was stopped for violating N.C. Gen. Stat. § 20-154(a) by failing to signal a lane change. The trial court found that at the time defendant's vehicle changed lanes without a signal, it was being operated by defendant immediately in front of the arresting officer's vehicle. That finding indicated that defendant's failure to signal violated § 20-154(a) because it was clear that changing lanes immediately in front of another vehicle might affect the operation of the trailing vehicle. The officer's observation of the traffic violation gave him the required reasonable suspicion to stop defendant's vehicle. Thus, the trial court's findings of fact supported its conclusion of law that defendant's constitutional rights were not violated by the stop.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the appellate court's decision.


 

47 of 825 DOCUMENTS



State v. Murrell


No. 484A06


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


362 N.C. 375; 665 S.E.2d 61; 2008 N.C. LEXIS 688


May 6, 2008, Heard in the Supreme Court 
August 27, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Murrell v. N.C., 2009 U.S. LEXIS 3025 (U.S., Apr. 20, 2009)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] NO ERROR; MOTION FOR APPROPRIATE RELIEF DENIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury found defendant guilty of first-degree murder, first-degree kidnapping, and robbery with a dangerous weapon. The Superior Court, Forsyth County (North Carolina) sentenced defendant to death for the murder. Defendant appealed as of right pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a) and filed a motion for appropriate relief.


OVERVIEW: Among other things, the supreme court concluded that the trial court did not err in concluding that defendant's waiver of his Miranda rights was knowing and voluntary and that his statement made to investigators, after defendant initiated a meeting, was admissible. The supreme court also found that the prosecutor's remarks addressing mitigating circumstances during voir dire were substantially correct and did not affect the impartiality of potential jurors. As for defendant's claims of error related to the prosecution's closing arguments, the supreme court found, inter alia, that the prosecutor's arguments were well within the "wide latitude" of what was permissible. The trial court's instructions on mitigating and aggravating factors were not improper, as the trial court submitted the mitigating factor of defendant's age to the jury and an instruction on pecuniary gain did not relieve the prosecution of its burden of proof. Finally, in denying defendant's motion to appropriate relief, the supreme court concluded that, inter alia, an allegedly perjured statement made by a witness for the State was in fact true and a jury instruction on accomplice testimony was not required.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed. Defendant's motion for appropriate relief was denied.


 

48 of 825 DOCUMENTS



Standley v. Town of Woodfin


No. 531A07


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


362 N.C. 328; 661 S.E.2d 728; 2008 N.C. LEXIS 496


May 5, 2008, Heard in the Supreme Court 
June 12, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Standley v. Town of Woodfin, 186 N.C. App. 134, 650 S.E.2d 618, 2007 N.C. App. LEXIS 2121 (N.C. Ct. App., 2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff sex offender filed suit against a town alleging that Woodfin, N.C., Ordinance § 130.03 (2005) violated the due process right to travel under the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution and N.C. Const. art. I, §§ 19 and 35. The North Carolina Court of Appeals (intermediate court) affirmed a summary judgment entered for the town. The sex offender appealed as of right.


OVERVIEW: The state's highest court noted that § 130.03 prohibited registered sex offenders from knowingly entering any public park owned, operated, or maintained by the town. The sex offender's alleged liberty interest to enter a park to have barbecues and enjoy nature along the riverbank was not protected by the right to intrastate travel as it was not a right of function upon which one would depend to carry out one's daily life activities. The sex offender's asserted liberty interest to freely roam in such parks was not a fundamental right as it was not so deeply rooted in the Nation's history and tradition and implicit in the concept of ordered liberty that neither liberty nor justice would exist if it were sacrificed. The town had been delegated the authority to prohibit acts detrimental to its citizens' health, safety, or welfare by N.C. Gen. Stat. § 160A-174 (2007). Protecting children and other visitors to parks owned and operated by the town from sexual attacks was a legitimate government interest. N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-208.5 (2007) recognized that sex offenders posed a high risk of engaging in sex offenses. Section 130.03 was rationally related to a legitimate government interest.


OUTCOME: The intermediate court's decision was affirmed.


 

49 of 825 DOCUMENTS



State v. Barnard


No. 347A07


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


362 N.C. 244; 658 S.E.2d 643; 2008 N.C. LEXIS 338


February 13, 2008, Heard in the Supreme Court 
April 11, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition denied by Barnard v. N.C., 2008 U.S. LEXIS 6113 (U.S., Oct. 6, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Barnard, 184 N.C. App. 25, 645 S.E.2d 780, 2007 N.C. App. LEXIS 1307 (2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, after his motion to suppress evidence was denied, was convicted by a jury in the trial court of two counts of possession of cocaine. Defendant pleaded guilty to one count of having achieved habitual felon status. A divided panel of the Court of Appeals of North Carolina found no error. Defendant appealed pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-30(2).


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that his constitutional rights were violated by the traffic stop that led to his convictions. In the traffic stop, a police officer, who was on patrol in a high crime area of a town where a number of bars were located, stopped his marked police car behind defendant's vehicle at a red traffic light. When the light turned green, defendant remained stopped for approximately 30 seconds before making a legal left turn. The officer then initiated the stop of the vehicle. The state supreme court reviewed defendant's challenge to the stop of his vehicle pursuant to U.S. Const. amend. IV and N.C. Const. art. I, § 20. The court found that, because defendant's 30-second delay at a green traffic light under the circumstances gave rise to a reasonable, articulable suspicion that he may have been driving while impaired, the stop of defendant's vehicle was constitutional and the evidence obtained as a result of the stop was properly admitted. It was irrelevant that part of the officer's motivation for stopping defendant may have been a perceived, though apparently nonexistent, statutory violation of impeding traffic.


OUTCOME: The decision of the intermediate appellate court was affirmed.


 

50 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Raines


No. 211A06


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


362 N.C. 1; 653 S.E.2d 126; 2007 N.C. LEXIS 1233


October 15, 2007, Heard in the Supreme Court 
December 7, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion denied by State v. Raines, 362 N.C. 179, 657 S.E.2d 374, 2007 N.C. LEXIS 1340 (2007)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Raines v. N.C., 2009 U.S. LEXIS 4957 (U.S., June 29, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Raines, 645 S.E.2d 378, 2007 N.C. LEXIS 397 (N.C., 2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed as of right, pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a), from judgments imposing a sentence of death entered by the Superior Court, Henderson County (North Carolina), upon jury verdicts finding him guilty of two counts of first-degree murder. The court allowed defendant's motion to bypass the appellate court as to his appeal of an additional judgment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's conviction arose from the double homicide of a married couple, who had taken defendant in at the age of 12 and raised him. Defendant viciously beat the couple with a wrench and stole their credit cards, property, and money. Defendant was arrested after selling some of the couple's property. Defendant raised various issues in his appeal, but the court found no reversible error. As to defendant's assignments of error with regard to voir dire, the court noted that defendant never exhausted all of his peremptory challenges, therefore, he failed to show any prejudice. The court found that even if a statement by the prosecutor during closing argument was improper, it was not so grossly improper so as to have rendered the trial and conviction unfair. The court found that the jury verdict form was not improper or misleading and there was no indication that the jury was confused. The admission of detention center incident reports during the penalty phase of the trial did not violate defendant's Confrontation Clause rights since the statements therein were not testimonial and were admissible under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 8C-1, N.C. R. Civ. P. 803(6) (2005).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and the imposition of the death penalty.

 

51 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Oglesby


No. 683PA05


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


361 N.C. 550; 648 S.E.2d 819; 2007 N.C. LEXIS 812


May 7, 2007, Heard in the Supreme Court 
August 24, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at State v. Jaamall Denaris Oglesby, 2007 N.C. App. LEXIS 2232 (N.C. Ct. App., Nov. 6, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Oglesby, 174 N.C. App. 658, 622 S.E.2d 152, 2005 N.C. App. LEXIS 2624 (2005)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED IN PART; VACATED AND REMANDED IN PART; DISCRETIONARY REVIEW IMPROVIDENTLY ALLOWED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After defendant's motion to suppress an incriminating statement he made to law enforcement officers was denied, a jury convicted him of first-degree murder, first-degree kidnapping, and attempted robbery with a firearm. The Court of Appeals of North Carolina found no error in part and remanded the case in part for resentencing. Both parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: A custodial interrogation took place when defendant was 16 years old. Defendant argued that his juvenile rights were violated during the interrogation because the detectives did not cease questioning him when he requested to phone his aunt. The trial court found, inter alia, that defendant's aunt was not his guardian or custodian under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7B-2101. In order to prevent manifest injustice, the appellate court decided to review the issue despite the fact that defendant failed to properly preserve the issue for review. An "aunt" was not an enumerated relation in N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7B-2101, and an interpretation of the term "guardian" to encompass anything other than a relationship established by legal process would have unjustifiably expanded the plain and unambiguous meaning of the word. As the trial court's finding of fact that the aunt was not defendant's guardian was supported by competent evidence, it was not disturbed on appeal.


OUTCOME: The decision of the intermediate appellate court which found no error in the convictions was affirmed. The portion of the decision in which that court found Blakely error in defendant's aggravated sentences for robbery with a dangerous weapon, which it treated as structural error, was vacated and the case was remanded for harmless error review.

State v. Cummings


No. 1A05


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


361 N.C. 438; 648 S.E.2d 788; 2007 N.C. LEXIS 815


January 8, 2007, Heard in the Supreme Court 
August 24, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Cummings v. N.C., 2008 U.S. LEXIS 3255 (U.S., Apr. 14, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Cummings, 647 S.E.2d 431, 2007 N.C. LEXIS 581 (N.C., 2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, as of right pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a), appealed the judgment imposing a sentence of death upon him by the Superior Court, New Hanover County (North Carolina), following a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's conviction and sentence arose from the stabbing death of his neighbor with a paring knife. Defendant stole the victim's bank card and withdrew money from her account. On appeal, defendant asserted that the trial court committed error with regard to denying his challenge for cause with regard to a juror who was a chief of detectives for a police department. The court found no error, concluding that the trial court record supported the trial court's conclusion that the juror performed his duties consistent with the trial court's instructions as to considering mitigating evidence and, further, the juror never indicated that he would automatically give more weight to any particular testimony. Likewise, the trial court did not err by refusing defendant's challenge for cause regarding a juror who defendant asserted would vote automatically for the death penalty since the trial court made extensive inquiry into the issue. In applying a plain error analysis to certain other asserted errors, the court found no reason to disturb the conviction or sentence.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that defendant received a fair trial and sentencing proceeding and found no error in his convictions or his sentences. The court also concluded that defendant's sentence of death was not disproportionate to the crime he committed and, therefore, affirmed defendant's conviction and death sentence.


 

53 of 825 DOCUMENTS



Pender County v. Bartlett


No. 103A06


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


361 N.C. 491; 649 S.E.2d 364; 2007 N.C. LEXIS 816


September 13, 2006, Heard in the Supreme Court 
August 24, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari granted by Bartlett v. Strickland, 128 S. Ct. 1648, 170 L. Ed. 2d 352, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 2446 (U.S., 2008)
Affirmed by Bartlett v. Strickland, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 1842 (U.S., Mar. 9, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: Pender County v. Bartlett, 633 S.E.2d 90, 2006 N.C. LEXIS 192 (N.C., 2006)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] REVERSED.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff county commissioners brought an action against defendant state officials in which plaintiffs argued that a redistricting plan violated the Whole County Provision (WCP), N.C. Const. art. II, §§ 3(3), 5(3). A three-judge panel of the Superior Court, Wake County (North Carolina), issued a summary judgment order holding that the plan was required by § 2 of the Voting Rights Act of 1965 (VRA), 42 U.S.C.S. § 1973. Plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: According to the United States Supreme Court, a minority group had to be sufficiently large and geographically compact to constitute a majority in a single-member district before a legislature was required to draw a legislative district under § 1973. The court held that the proper statistic for deciding whether a minority group met the precondition was voting age population as refined by citizenship. Furthermore, a bright line rule was appropriate: thus, if a minority group lacked a numerical majority of citizens of voting age, the precondition had not been satisfied and it was not necessary to create a § 1973 legislative district. The district in question here did not meet this bright line test. Thus, because the district was not required by § 1973, it had to be drawn in accordance with the WCP and the requirements set forth by the court in Stephenson I. The district here did not comply with these criteria. One of the counties in question lacked sufficient population to support a non-VRA house district; thus, a voting district which included that county had to add population across a county line, but only to the extent necessary to comply with the one-person, one vote standard.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's decision.


 

54 of 825 DOCUMENTS



State v. Badgett


No. 522A04


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


361 N.C. 234; 644 S.E.2d 206; 2007 N.C. LEXIS 416


September 11, 2006, Heard in the Supreme Court  
May 4, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Badgett v. North Carolina, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 11865 (U.S., Oct. 29, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Badgett, 633 S.E.2d 472, 2006 N.C. LEXIS 694 (N.C., 2006)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] NO PREJUDICIAL ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was indicted for armed robbery and first-degree murder. Defendant's conviction for first-degree murder was based on a theory of malice, premeditation, and deliberation, and the felony murder rule. Following a capital sentencing proceeding, a jury recommended a sentence of death. The Superior Court, Randolph County (North Carolina) entered judgment accordingly and arrested judgment on the robbery conviction. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred by denying his motion to exclude evidence related to a prior killing. The supreme court concluded that the evidence satisfied both the similarity and temporal requirements of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 8C-1, N.C. R. Evid. 404(b), as, inter alia, both crimes involved a fatal stab wound to an unarmed victim's neck with a folding pocketknife, which occurred during an argument in the victim's home. While the supreme court found it was error to admit the detective's testimony regarding the prior crime since defendant did not testify, the supreme court further found the error harmless in light of the myriad of evidence that defendant committed the prior crime. Any error that occurred when a bailiff told jurors not to discuss the case or read any media accounts was harmless as, inter alia, the communication did not related to defendant's guilt or innocence. The trial court's refusal to submit certain mitigating circumstances to the jury was not erroneous, as there was no evidence defendant suffered from any mental or emotional disturbance at the time of the crime. Finally, the supreme court concluded that the death penalty was proportionate to the crime.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.


 

55 of 825 DOCUMENTS


In re R.L.C.


No. 531A06


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


361 N.C. 287; 643 S.E.2d 920; 2007 N.C. LEXIS 417


February 15, 2007, Heard in the Supreme Court  
May 4, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition denied by R. L. C. v. N.C., 2007 U.S. LEXIS 12236 (U.S., Nov. 13, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: In re R.L.C., 179 N.C. App. 311, 635 S.E.2d 1, 2006 N.C. App. LEXIS 1913 (2006)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile sought review of a decision of a divided panel of the Court of Appeals (North Carolina) which found no error in juvenile adjudication and disposition orders entered by the trial court. The issue was whether appellant could be adjudicated delinquent based upon violating the crime against nature statute. Appellant argued he could not be adjudicated delinquent because he was not more than three years older than his girlfriend.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, who was 14, was dating a girl who was 12. During the relationship, the two juveniles had sexual intercourse and engaged in two separate incidents of fellatio. The Supreme Court of North Carolina noted that the statutes that contained age differentials did not constrain appellant's sexual activity in the present instance. However, the crime against nature statute, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-177, did. Accordingly, appellant's actions violated the crime against nature statute, which did not contain any age differential element. Besides the goal of promoting proper notions of morality among the State's youth, the government's desire for a healthy young citizenry underscored the legitimacy of the government's interest in prohibiting the commission of crimes against nature by minors. Like vaginal intercourse, non-vaginal sexual activity carried with it the risk of sexually transmitted diseases. Thus, the crime against nature statute did not run afoul of the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the lower appellate court was affirmed.

State v. Blackwell


No. 490PA04-2


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


361 N.C. 41; 638 S.E.2d 452; 2006 N.C. LEXIS 1301


October 17, 2006, Heard on reconsideration in the Supreme Court  
December 15, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Stay denied by, Motion denied by State v. Blackwell, 361 N.C. 222, 641 S.E.2d 801, 2007 N.C. LEXIS 6 (2007)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Blackwell v. North Carolina, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 5380 (U.S., May 14, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Blackwell, 359 N.C. 823, 620 S.E.2d 528, 2005 N.C. LEXIS 946 (2005)
State v. Blackwell, 359 N.C. 814, 618 S.E.2d 213, 2005 N.C. LEXIS 846 (2005)
State v. Blackwell, 166 N.C. App. 280, 603 S.E.2d 168, 2004 N.C. App. LEXIS 1694 (2004)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED IN PART AND REVERSED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted and sentenced in the trial court for second-degree murder, felonious habitual impaired driving, felonious assault with a deadly weapon inflicting serious injury, and misdemeanor assault with a deadly weapon. The Court of Appeals of North Carolina remanded defendant's case to the trial court for resentencing. The State of North Carolina filed a petition for discretionary review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who was driving a vehicle while under the influence of alcohol and narcotics, was involved in an auto accident with one fatality and personal injuries. The trial court found as an aggravating factor that defendant committed each felony while he was on pretrial release for another charge. Defendant contended that the trial court's failure to submit the aggravating factor in N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-1340.16(d)(12) (2005) to the jury was not harmless under U.S. Const. amend. VI. On appeal, the court found that the trial court possessed the authority to submit the aggravating factor to the jury using a special verdict in compliance with the aforementioned constitutional limitations. Thus, defendant's argument that the trial court lacked a procedural mechanism by which to submit the challenged aggravating factor to the jury was without merit. Moreover, the evidence presented at defendant's trial, showing that he committed the underlying crime while on pretrial release, was both uncontroverted and overwhelming. Finally, the trial court's finding of the aggravating factor at defendant's trial was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt, and did not violate N.C. Const. art. I, § 24.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was reversed to the extent that the intermediate appellate court ordered remand of defendant's case for resentencing. The opinion of the intermediate appellate court remained undisturbed in all other respects.

 

57 of 825 DOCUMENTS



Goldston v. State


No. 328PA04-2


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


361 N.C. 26; 637 S.E.2d 876; 2006 N.C. LEXIS 1300


October 16, 2006, Heard in the Supreme Court  
December 15, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at, Decision reached on appeal by Goldston v. State, 2009 N.C. App. LEXIS 1564 (N.C. Ct. App., Sept. 15, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: Goldston v. State, 173 N.C. App. 416, 618 S.E.2d 785, 2005 N.C. App. LEXIS 2012 (2005)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers, on behalf of themselves and other citizens similarly situated, sued defendants State and governor, alleging that money transfers from the North Carolina Highway Trust Fund to the general fund were illegal. The trial court granted summary judgment for the State and governor while denying summary judgment for the taxpayers. The Court of Appeals (North Carolina) affirmed. The taxpayers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers sought a declaration that the actions were illegal and unconstitutional. The trial court dismissed the case for lack of standing. The appellate court disagreed. The claim of illegal and unconstitutional diversion of funds derived from taxes paid by the taxpayers and others similarly situated was an actual controversy. A declaratory judgment would have served to clarify and settle the legal rights and responsibilities of the governor and the State, as well as the legal status of the taxpayer funds in the Highway Trust Fund. A declaratory judgment also would have terminated the uncertainty and controversy giving rise to the action. Accordingly, the taxpayers had standing to seek equitable relief and a declaratory judgment. Declaratory judgment remained an appropriate remedy here. A declaration as to the legality and constitutionality of the governor's and the State's diversions from the Trust Fund may well have been the most assured and effective remedy available.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was reversed. The case was remanded to the intermediate appellate court for further remand to the trial court.

State v. Ivey


No. 458PA05


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


360 N.C. 562; 633 S.E.2d 459; 2006 N.C. LEXIS 844


April 18, 2006, Heard in the Supreme Court  
August 18, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by State v. Ivey, 360 N.C. 655, 636 S.E.2d 573, 2006 N.C. LEXIS 998 (2006)
Motion denied by State v. Ivey, 360 N.C. 655, 636 S.E.2d 573, 2006 N.C. LEXIS 999 (2006)
Stay denied by State v. Ivey, 360 N.C. 655, 636 S.E.2d 573, 2006 N.C. LEXIS 1002 (2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Ivey, 171 N.C. App. 516, 615 S.E.2d 738, 2005 N.C. App. LEXIS 1406 (2005)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following the denial of defendant's motion in limine to exclude a firearm from evidence, defendant pleaded guilty to possession of a firearm and carrying a concealed weapon. The trial court sentenced defendant, who had a prior record of level II, at the maximum of the presumptive range to a term of 15 to 18 months imprisonment. The Court of Appeals (North Carolina) affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was stopped after making a right turn without using a signal. On appeal, defendant challenged the constitutionality of the traffic stop leading to defendant's arrest, alleging that the officer lacked probable cause to believe defendant's operation of the vehicle violated any traffic laws. The supreme court held that a reasonable officer, under the circumstances presented, would not have had probable cause to believe that a traffic violation occurred and, thus, the seizure and subsequent search of defendant's vehicle were unreasonable and violated defendant's rights under the Fourth Amendment and N.C. Const. art. I, § 20. The supreme court noted that the record did not indicate that any other vehicles or pedestrians were, or might have been affected by defendant's turn. Thus, the only question was whether the officer's vehicle might have been affected by the turn, which the supreme court concluded it was not. Defendant turned right, the only legal movement possible at the intersection and the officer's only option was to stop at the intersection, where there was a stop sign. Thus, the firearm recovered as a result of the search should have been suppressed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the Court of Appeals was reversed and the case was remanded with instructions to vacate defendant's convictions and remand to the trial court for further proceedings.

State v. Norris


No. 486A05


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


360 N.C. 507; 630 S.E.2d 915; 2006 N.C. LEXIS 592


February 15, 2006, Heard in the Supreme Court  
June 30, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Norris v. N.C., 2006 U.S. LEXIS 8800 (U.S., Nov. 27, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Norris, 172 N.C. App. 722, 617 S.E.2d 298, 2005 N.C. App. LEXIS 1787 (2005)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by a jury in the trial court of first-degree arson. The trial court weighed one aggravating factor against three mitigating factors and sentenced defendant to imprisonment for 51 to 71 months. The Court of Appeals of North Carolina remanded the case for resentencing, with instructions to submit any aggravating factor to the jury. The State of North Carolina appealed. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the appeal.


OVERVIEW: The trial court weighed one aggravating factor, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-1340.16(d)(8) (2005), against three mitigating factors, under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-1340.16(e), and sentenced defendant to a sentence within the presumptive range. Defendant argued that his Sixth Amendment right to a jury trial was violated by the trial court not submitting the aggravating factor to the jury. The majority of the intermediate appellate court ruled that the sentence was unconstitutional because the judge, not the jury, found the aggravating factor. The state supreme court found that it could hear the appeal because N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-30(2) (2005) provided the State of North Carolina with an appeal of right as there was a dissent in the intermediate appellate court. Further, nothing in N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-1422 prohibited the state supreme court from addressing the issues presented in defendant's brief to the intermediate appellate court. As to whether defendant's Sixth Amendment right to a jury trial was violated, the court found that the trial court inflicted punishment within the presumptive range, and consequently, its finding of an aggravating factor did not implicate the Sixth Amendment.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion to dismiss was denied, and the decision of the intermediate appellate court was reversed.

 

60 of 825 DOCUMENTS


Coley v. State


No. 607A05


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


360 N.C. 493; 631 S.E.2d 121; 2006 N.C. LEXIS 594


March 13, 2006, Heard in the Supreme Court  
June 30, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Coley v. State, 360 N.C. 582, 635 S.E.2d 430, 2006 N.C. LEXIS 857 (N.C., Aug. 17, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: Coley v. State, 173 N.C. App. 481, 620 S.E.2d 25, 2005 N.C. App. LEXIS 2104 (2005)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers filed a purported class action against appellees, the State of North Carolina and the North Carolina Secretary of Revenue, for a judgment declaring that 2001 N.C. Sess. Laws 424, § 34.18(b) violated N.C. Const. art. I, § 16. The trial court denied the taxpayers' motion for summary judgment and allowed the State's and the Secretary's motion to dismiss. The Court of Appeals of North Carolina affirmed. The taxpayers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers contended that 2001 N.C. Sess. Laws 424, § 34.18(b) was retrospective because it taxed income-producing acts previously done before the date of enactment. The state supreme court found that the imposition of a tax on income was a tax on an "other act" under N.C. Const. art. I, § 16. However, 2001 N.C. Sess. Laws 424, as codified in N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-134.2(a) (2005), did not tax the taxpayers retrospectively. The subject of the enacted tax was the North Carolina taxable income of an individual taxpayer, which was computed on the basis of the taxable year under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-134.4 (2005). Regardless of whether the taxable year was determined by a taxpayer's annual accounting period or by the calendar year, the taxable income and corresponding tax liability or overpayment were not fixed until the close of that year. Thus, because the taxpayers' taxable income was not fixed at the date of enactment, the midyear tax rate increase implemented by 2001 N.C. Sess. Laws 424 was not levied until the conclusion of the taxable year. Consequently, the tax operated prospectively from the date of enactment and did not violate N.C. Const. art. I, § 16.


OUTCOME: The opinion of the intermediate appellate court, affirming the trial court's grant of the motion to dismiss, was affirmed as modified.

State v. Forte


No. 20A04


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


360 N.C. 427; 629 S.E.2d 137; 2006 N.C. LEXIS 45


November 14, 2005, Heard in the Supreme Court  
May 5, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected June 27, 2006.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Forte v. North Carolina, 549 U.S. 1021, 127 S. Ct. 557, 166 L. Ed. 2d 413, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 8422 (2006)
Writ denied by State v. Forte, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 46 (N.C., Feb. 5, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Forte, 619 S.E.2d 853, 2005 N.C. LEXIS 889 (N.C., 2005)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] NO ERROR GUILT-INNOCENCE PHASE; NO ERROR CAPITAL SENTENCING PROCEEDING; NON-CAPITAL SENTENCING VACATED AND REMANDED FOR RESENTENCING.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Superior Court, Wayne County, North Carolina, convicted defendant of first degree rape and first degree murder, in addition to other crimes, finding that he raped and killed three elderly women and killed the husband of one of them; that woman was blind. He appealed his convictions and his three death sentences, claiming in part that the trial court violated his right of confrontation by admitting certain documentary evidence.


OVERVIEW: State investigative reports were admitted even though the person who made the reports did not testify; they described DNA test results, and his supervisor testified. The court ruled in part that the reports were properly admitted as business records under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 8C-1, N.C. R. Evid. 803(6) (2005), as they were not testimonial but were neutral and were not prepared for trial. Further, they were admissible under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 8C-1, N.C. R. Evid. 803(8), as public records; they concerned routine matters and recorded only ministerial observations. His statements to the police were admissible and did not violate his rights because his response as to whether he wanted to be questioned was ambiguous. There was no error in failing to submit to the jury his background as a mitigating factor under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(f)(1); his record before the murders showed many prior convictions. Thus, while it was error to consider his history after the murders, the error was harmless. There was no prosecutorial error, even though the argument as to the couple's deaths was graphic; the prosecutor drew reasonable inferences from the facts. The death sentences were proportional.


OUTCOME: The court overruled defendant's assignments of error, but found no error in the guilt and innocence phase and no error in the capital sentencing proceeding. Defendant's non-capital sentences were vacated and remanded for resentencing.

State v. Elliott


No. 184A04


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


360 N.C. 400; 628 S.E.2d 735; 2006 N.C. LEXIS 46


March 13, 2006, Heard in the Supreme Court  
May 5, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Elliott v. North Carolina, 549 U.S. 1000, 127 S. Ct. 505, 166 L. Ed. 2d 378, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 8127 (2006)
Motion granted by State v. Elliott, 690 S.E.2d 276, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 5 (N.C., 2010)
Writ denied by State v. Elliott, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 762 (N.C., Oct. 7, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Elliott, 629 S.E.2d 610, 2006 N.C. LEXIS 566 (N.C., 2006)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Superior Court, Moore County, North Carolina, convicted defendant of first-degree murder, first-degree rape, and first-degree murder and made a binding recommendation of a sentence of death on the murder convictions. Defendant filed a motion for appropriate relief from a judgment imposing a sentence of death, which was denied. He appealed, raising over 20 assignments of error, many focusing on the jury and the jury instructions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant complained that a juror was more than 65 years old. The court on review ruled that under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 9-6.1 (2001), the juror was qualified and that excusing her was in a trial court's discretion. It was no error to refuse to allow defendant to question prospective jurors about their death penalty attitudes; the trial court had the discretion to deny the request, and he showed no prejudice. Further, a juror was not excluded because he or she had an opinion about the death penalty. The trial court also allowed him to ask the jurors if they would apply the law as given. There no error in restoring some peremptory challenges as the trial court did not add to them; an entire venire was dismissed after it was learned that they discussed how to be excused, and the challenges replaced those that were already used. There was no juror misconduct when two jurors prayed outside the jury room. Nothing showed pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-1420(b) that they discussed the case when praying. Thus, defendant had no right to relief on his motion, pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-1420(c)(6). Giving the heinous or atrocious instruction was not error. The sentence was proportionate.


OUTCOME: The court found no error and overruled defendant's assignments of error.

State v. Allen


No. 115A04


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


360 N.C. 297; 626 S.E.2d 271; 2006 N.C. LEXIS 23


September 14, 2005, Heard in the Supreme Court  
March 3, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Allen v. North Carolina, 127 S. Ct. 164, 166 L. Ed. 2d 116, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 6945 (U.S., Oct. 2, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Allen, 618 S.E.2d 236, 2005 N.C. LEXIS 798 (N.C., 2005)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Superior Court, Montgomery County (North Carolina) of murder, felonious larceny, and felonious possession of stolen goods. Defendant was sentenced to death and appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the prosecution violated his right to a fair trial by knowingly offering false statements of an eyewitness. The supreme court disagreed. The length of time it took the victim to die was not easily proved. Although the medical examiner testified that the victim would not have survived as long as the witness testified--seven to eight hours--that testimony was his medical opinion. It could not be said that the eyewitness's statement was knowingly false when elicited. In fact, during closing arguments, the prosecution admitted that the eyewitness's perception of time "might not have been correct." Similarly, the testimony about the "emptying" of the victim's handgun, while unlikely to be accurate, could not be said to have been known as false by the State. The witness was a confessed drug addict and under the influence of drugs at the time of the murder. That, along with her prior convictions and other circumstances of her lifestyle revealed at defendant's trial, made her a witness with less-than-perfect credibility. The prosecution could have believed that the eyewitness was simply mistaken and did not hear as many shots as she thought.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

64 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. McNeill


No. 615A03


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


360 N.C. 231; 624 S.E.2d 329; 2006 N.C. LEXIS 1


October 17, 2005, Heard in the Supreme Court  
January 27, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by McNeill v. North Carolina, 549 U.S. 960, 127 S. Ct. 396, 166 L. Ed. 2d 281, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 7615 (2006)
Writ denied by State v. McNeill, 2009 N.C. LEXIS 900 (N.C., Oct. 8, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. McNeill, 618 S.E.2d 738, 2005 N.C. LEXIS 818 (N.C., 2005)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury found defendant guilty of first-degree murder on the basis of malice, premeditation, and deliberation, and under the felony murder rule, and of discharging a firearm into occupied property. The jury returned a binding recommendation of death for the murder conviction. The Superior Court, Scotland County (North Carolina) sentenced defendant to death for the murder and 34 to 50 months for the firearm conviction. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: In response to defendant's multiple assignments of error, the appellate court concluded that: (1) the State presented sufficient evidence, including the multiplicity of gun shots inflicted in rapid succession, defendant's disregard of the victim's plea for life, the victim's realization she was about to be killed, and defendant's calmness and lack of regret, to support submission of the especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel (HAC) aggravating circumstance to the jury; (2) the prosecutor's recitation of only a portion of the HAC pattern jury instruction did not constitute gross error but aided the jury in understanding what had been held to be the types of murders in which HAC could be found; (3) defendant was properly precluded from making comparisons between other HAC cases and the facts of each case in closing arguments; (4) the trial court properly denied defendant's request to change the language in a jury instruction regarding mitigating and aggravating circumstances because the instruction proffered by defendant was an incorrect statement of the law and the instruction given did not erroneously shift the burden of proof; and (5) defendant's sentence was not disproportionate.


OUTCOME: The appellate court found no error in the judgment of the trial court.

State v. Hurst


No. 363A04


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


360 N.C. 181; 624 S.E.2d 309; 2006 N.C. LEXIS 7


October 19, 2005, Heard in the Supreme Court  
January 27, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Hurst v. North Carolina, 549 U.S. 875, 127 S. Ct. 186, 166 L. Ed. 2d 131, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 7059 (2006)
Writ of certiorari denied State v. Hurst, 364 N.C. 244, 698 S.E.2d 664, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 422 (2010)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Stay denied by Hurst v. Branker, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 53567 (M.D.N.C., May 18, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Hurst, 615 S.E.2d 863, 2005 N.C. LEXIS 631 (N.C., 2005)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction by the Superior Court, Randolph County (North Carolina) for first-degree murder both on the basis of malice, premeditation and deliberation and on the basis of felony murder, which resulted in the death penalty. Defendant argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred in failing to declare a mistrial after a prospective alternate juror brought a newspaper article about the trial into the jury room.


OVERVIEW: Defendant stated that he knew he was going to kill the victim the day before the murder. Defendant shot the victim three times with a shotgun. Defendant said that the victim had not provoked him and declined to give a reason for the shooting. The appellate court held the evidence supported the trial court's finding that none of the 12 jurors selected for the sitting panel were in the jury room by the time the article appeared there and that defendant had not shown the substantial and irreparable harm required by N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-1061 for declaration of a mistrial. Moreover, none of the alternate jurors participated in the deliberations at defendant's trial. Thus, even if alternate jurors were exposed to the article, any resulting taint was immaterial and caused defendant no prejudice. No rational jury could find that defendant's criminal activity was insignificant. Accordingly, the trial court did not err in deciding not to instruct pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(f)(1). The record demonstrated that his maturity was consistent with his chronological age. The trial court did not err in declining to submit the N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(f)(7) mitigating circumstance.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

State v. Berryman


No. 302A05


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


360 N.C. 209; 624 S.E.2d 350; 2006 N.C. LEXIS 8


October 17, 2005, Heard in the Supreme Court  
January 27, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Berryman, 170 N.C. App. 336, 612 S.E.2d 672, 2005 N.C. App. LEXIS 1004 (N.C. Ct. App., 2005)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury found defendant guilty of common law robbery, possession of cocaine, and being an habitual felon. The trial court sentenced defendant to a prison term of 133 months to 169 months. Defendant appealed. The Court of Appeals upheld the trial court's decision. Pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-30(2), defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The issue before the supreme court was whether a six-year delay in the preparation of a trial transcript for appellate review violated defendant's constitutional or statutory right to an appeal. The supreme court held that defendant's statutory and due process rights were not violated by the six-year delay in producing his trial transcript. The supreme court noted that defendant bore the burden of proceeding and of ensuring that the record on appeal and verbatim transcript were complete, properly settled, in correct form, and filed with the appropriate appellate court by the applicable deadlines. In addition, the supreme court noted that, contrary to defendant's assertion, the burden was on defendant to show that the delay resulted from intentional conduct by the State. The supreme court agreed with the court of appeals that the record was devoid of any indication as to why the delay took place. The record included, inter alia, evidence that defense counsel made approximately nine inquiries to the court reporter regarding the transcript during the first two years, but there was a noticeable gap of about three years during which no inquiries were made.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court was affirmed.

State v. Duke


No. 57A04


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


360 N.C. 110; 623 S.E.2d 11; 2005 N.C. LEXIS 1314


October 18, 2005, Heard in the Supreme Court  
December 16, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Duke v. North Carolina, 127 S. Ct. 130, 166 L. Ed. 2d 96, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 6725 (U.S., 2006)
Motion denied by State v. Duke, 362 N.C. 178, 657 S.E.2d 667, 2008 N.C. LEXIS 39 (N.C., Jan. 24, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Duke, 616 S.E.2d 924, 2005 N.C. LEXIS 652 (N.C., 2005)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After retrial, the Superior Court, Gaston County, North Carolina, upon jury verdicts, convicted defendant of two counts of first-degree murder and imposed consecutive death sentences. Defendant appealed as of right pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a).


OVERVIEW: The jury's finding that three aggravating circumstances existed beyond a reasonable doubt as to both murders was supported by the record and was not the result of passion, prejudice, or other arbitrary factor. Defendant was previously convicted of a felony involving the use of violence to the person under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(e)(3). The murders were especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(e)(9). The murders were part of a course of conduct in which defendant engaged and which included the commission by him of other crimes of violence against other persons under N.C. Gen. No juror found either statutory mitigating circumstance as to either murder, but at least one juror found 11 nonstatutory mitigating circumstances as to each murder. The highest court did not disturb the jury's weighing of the mitigating and aggravating circumstances under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(c) and found the death sentences proportionate, emphasizing the heinous, atrocious, or cruel mitigator and the double murder.


OUTCOME: The highest court affirmed the conviction and the two consecutive death sentences.

State v. Campbell


No. 366A02


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


359 N.C. 644; 617 S.E.2d 1; 2005 N.C. LEXIS 842


April 13, 2004, Heard in the Supreme Court  
August 19, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition denied by Campbell v. N.C., 547 U.S. 1073, 126 S. Ct. 1773, 164 L. Ed. 2d 523, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 3220 (U.S., Apr. 17, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
State v. Campbell, 589 S.E.2d 727, 2003 N.C. LEXIS 1331 (N.C., 2003)



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed as of right pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered in the Superior Court, Pender County (North Carolina), upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court found that defendant was not illegally seized in an encounter with an officer as an officer did not tell defendant he could not leave, defendant had consented to speak with the officer, and the officer had not restrained defendant's freedom to walk away. OUTCOME: No error was found, and the judgment of the trial court was left undisturbed.


 

69 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Jones


No. 389PA04


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


359 N.C. 832; 616 S.E.2d 496; 2005 N.C. LEXIS 845


March 14, 2005, Heard in the Supreme Court  
August 19, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Jones, 165 N.C. App. 540, 598 S.E.2d 694, 2004 N.C. App. LEXIS 1398 (2004)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was granted discretionary review of additional issues after the Court of Appeals (North Carolina), vacated a superior court judgment that convicted defendant on charges of assault by pointing a gun, assault with a deadly weapon with intent to kill inflicting serious injury, and attempted murder.


OVERVIEW: In the appellate court, defendant argued that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15-144 (2003), which authorized the use of a short-form murder indictment, did not support defendant's conviction for attempted murder. While the appellate court disagreed, it vacated the attempted murder conviction based on reasoning that the indictment charged the offense of attempted common law murder, which was not recognized by North Carolina's general statutes. The court reversed, rejecting in the first instance the finding that the indictment charged attempted common law murder. The indictment could only have been for attempted first-degree murder because it recited defendant's specific intent to commit murder. Construing § 15-144 in conjunction with N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15-170 (2003), the court then ruled that use of the short-form indictment for attempted first-degree murder was appropriate. Section 15-170 permitted an indictment for first-degree murder to sustain a conviction for attempted first-degree murder, and thus, the State was implicitly authorized to utilize the short-form indictment to actually charge attempted first-degree murder.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's judgment was reversed and the case was remanded to that court for further remand to the superior court for entry of judgment consistent with the supreme court's opinion.

 

70 of 825 DOCUMENTS



N.C. Sch. Bds. Ass'n v. Moore


No. 569A03


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


359 N.C. 474; 614 S.E.2d 504; 2005 N.C. LEXIS 694


May 11, 2004, Heard in the Supreme Court  
July 1, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: N.C. Sch. Bds. Ass'n v. Moore, 160 N.C. App. 253, 585 S.E.2d 418, 2003 N.C. App. LEXIS 1794 (2003)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED IN PART; REVERSED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, various State of North Carolina agencies and officials, appealed the decision of the Court of Appeals (North Carolina), which affirmed, in part, and reversed, in part, a trial court's order of summary judgment that addressed whether monetary payments to the state agencies were being retained in violation of N.C. Const. art. IX, § 7. Plaintiffs, various county school boards, had commenced the declaratory judgment action.


OVERVIEW: The main issue was whether certain monetary payments to the state agencies were being remitted to the State's General Fund or being retained by those agencies in violation of art. IX, § 7. The boards argued that the precedents held that any civil penalties paid for violation of a penal law of the State and accruing to the State were necessarily punitive and had to be paid to the public schools. Defendants argued that any penalty paid to the State to compensate it for an injury, damage, or loss above normal operating costs fell outside the scope of art. IX, § 7. The court agreed with the boards that any monies collected by the Department of Revenue for late filings or underpayments and for failing to comply with various tax provisions fell within the scope of art. IX, § 7 and were payable to the boards. The court also found that payments for traffic and parking funds collected by universities and payments made by environmental offenders were subject to art. IX, § 7. The excise tax on unauthorized substances was not a penalty subject to the provisions of art. IX, § 7 but penalties and interest monies for the collection of the same were. Other monies collected were also addressed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision as to the monies received for the excise tax on unauthorized substances. The court reversed the decision as to the monies collected for failing to comply with tax provisions, the penalties and interest collected for the excise tax on unauthorized substances, funds collected by universities for traffic and parking violations and from environmental offenders, and civil penalties paid by public schools.

State v. Allen


No. 485PA04


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


359 N.C. 425; 615 S.E.2d 256; 2005 N.C. LEXIS 695


March 15, 2005, Heard in the Supreme Court  
July 1, 2005, Filed


NOTICE:

 [***1] THIS OPINION WAS WITHDRAWN BY THE COURT


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Opinion withdrawn by State v. Allen, 2006 N.C. LEXIS 1012 (N.C., Aug. 17, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Allen, 166 N.C. App. 139, 601 S.E.2d 299, 2004 N.C. App. LEXIS 1603 (2004)



DISPOSITION: MODIFIED AND AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's petition for discretionary review under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-31 was granted regarding a decision by the Court of Appeals (North Carolina), which found no error in defendant's conviction for felony child abuse inflicting serious bodily injury but which remanded for resentencing.


OVERVIEW: A jury unanimously found defendant guilty of felony child abuse inflicting serious bodily injury. During sentencing, his sentence was aggravated on findings by the trial judge. He appealed, arguing his conviction was improper and that the provisions of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-1340.16(a), (b), (c), which allowed the trial court to find aggravating factors by a preponderance of the evidence, was unconstitutional. The lower appellate court found no error in the conviction but remanded for resentencing only. Defendant's petition for discretionary review was granted. The court found, first, that while N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-1422(f) normally barred such review, N.C. Const. art. IV, § 12, cl. 1, gave the court power to issue a decision since the lower appellate court's decision was of constitutional magnitude and widespread effect. The court affirmed the decision and agreed that those parts of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-1340.16 violated the rule of Blakely since U.S. Const. amend. VI required that the especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel aggravating factor be proved to a jury beyond a reasonable doubt and not merely found by a judge by the preponderance of the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision to resentence defendant, and the court modified the holding to clarify that the statute under which defendant's sentence was aggravated was unconstitutional.

State v. Bryant


No. 173PA04


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


359 N.C. 554; 614 S.E.2d 479; 2005 N.C. LEXIS 647


December 7, 2004, Heard in the Supreme Court  
July 1, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at State v. Bryant, 2006 N.C. App. LEXIS 1703 (N.C. Ct. App., Aug. 1, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Bryant, 163 N.C. App. 478, 594 S.E.2d 202, 2004 N.C. App. LEXIS 403 (2004)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] REVERSED and REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State filed petitions for a writ of supersedeas and for discretionary review with regard to the decision of the Court of Appeals (North Carolina), which reversed a judgment of a trial court that had convicted defendant of failing to register as a sex offender pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-208.11 (2003). Defendant was also found to have been a habitual offender.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a convicted sex offender from the State of South Carolina and was registered in that state. Defendant did not dispute that he moved to North Carolina wherein he lived with a girlfriend. Defendant was arrested in North Carolina for failing to register as a convicted sex offender in North Carolina. He was convicted and was also found to have attained the status of a habitual felon. Defendant sought to have § 14-208.11 declared unconstitutional based on allegedly insufficient notice of the existence of the criminal statute itself. The lower court had found that North Carolina's sex offender registration statute was unconstitutional as applied to an out-of-state offender who lacked notice of his duty to register upon moving to North Carolina. The court disagreed and held that § 14-208.11 was facially constitutional as applied to a convicted sex offender from another jurisdiction who subsequently moved to North Carolina, such as defendant. The court found that defendant had actual notice of his lifelong duty to register with South Carolina, which led a reasonable individual to inquire of a duty to register in any state upon relocation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the case to the lower court for consideration of the remainder of defendant's assignments of error not previously addressed.

State v. Thompson


No. 142A03


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


359 N.C. 77; 604 S.E.2d 850; 2004 N.C. LEXIS 1197


May 10, 2004, Heard in the Supreme Court  
December 3, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Thompson v. North Carolina, 546 U.S. 830, 126 S. Ct. 48, 163 L. Ed. 2d 80, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 6156 (2005)
Writ of certiorari denied State v. Thompson, 2012 N.C. LEXIS 1215 (N.C., Dec. 12, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Thompson, 587 S.E.2d 417, 2003 N.C. LEXIS 1130 (N.C., 2003)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury found defendant guilty of first-degree murder, robbery with a firearm, and burning of a building used in trade. The jury recommended a death sentence for the murder conviction. The Superior Court, Guilford County (North Carolina) sentenced defendant to death for the murder conviction, a term of 103 to 133 months for the robbery and 21 to 26 months for the burning conviction. Defendant appealed his death sentence to the state supreme court.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court held that defendant received a fair trial and capital sentencing proceeding, free of reversible error. Among other things, the supreme court found that the testimony of a social worker that the victim's family did not respond to her inquiries with respect to defendant's remorse was not an inadmissible victim impact statement. In addition, there was no error in the submission of aggravating and mitigating circumstances to the jury. The failure to instruct on the mitigating circumstance of lack of a significant history of prior criminal activity was properly based on evidence of defendant's prior robbery convictions, as no rational jury could have concluded that defendant had no significant history of prior criminal activity. Nor did the supreme court find that defense counsel improperly conceded to jurors during voir dire that defendant was guilty, thus depriving defendant of his right to effective assistance of counsel. In addition, with regard to statements made during trial, the record reflected that defendant consented to the trial strategy employed by his defense counsel. Finally, the supreme court found no merit in defendant's proportionality challenge.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder on the basis of premeditation and deliberation. The jury recommended a sentence of death and the Superior Court, Buncombe County (North Carolina) entered judgment accordingly. Defendant appealed as of right pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a) directly to the supreme court.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court found that the trial court properly denied defendant's motion for a continuance as to a pretrial hearing where the record established that substitute second chair counsel was adequately prepared to proceed; counsel filed numerous motions on defendant's behalf, met several times with the prosecutors, and engaged in aggressive and informed cross-examination. In addition, evidence of two prior assaults was properly admitted under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 8C-1, N.C. R. Evid. 403, 404(b), as such evidence demonstrated that defendant was aware that striking a person with a beer bottle was a reckless and dangerous act that could cause serious injury.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

State v. Bell


No. 86A02


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


359 N.C. 1; 603 S.E.2d 93; 2004 N.C. LEXIS 1126


May 11, 2004, Heard in the Supreme Court  
October 7, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Bell v. North Carolina, 544 U.S. 1052, 125 S. Ct. 2299, 161 L. Ed. 2d 1094, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 4243 (2005)
Stay granted by State v. Bell, 2013 N.C. LEXIS 1500 (N.C., Jan. 24, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Bell, 586 S.E.2d 102, 2003 N.C. LEXIS 850 (N.C., 2003)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court, Onslow County (North Carolina), convicted defendant of first-degree murder and kidnapping, and personal property burning. The jury found five aggravators: prior violent felony (robbery), avoiding arrest, first-degree kidnapping, pecuniary gain, and especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(e)(3), (4), (5), (6), (9), and recommended the death penalty, which the trial court imposed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The appeal bypassed the appeals court. Defendant, co-defendant, and the State's eyewitness, who pleaded guilty for a reduced sentence in exchange for his testimony, kidnapped an 89-year-old woman victim from her home, repeatedly beat her and placed in her car's trunk, where she was fighting to free herself, trapped for hours, helpless, in fear, and died when defendant set the car on fire, with her in it, from carbon monoxide poisoning. The jury found two statutory mitigators and six nonstatutory mitigating circumstances. The State had race-neutral reasons for exercising peremptory challenges. Defendant's and co-defendant's cases were properly joined, considering the same facts were involved. The State's analogies, references to jurors by name, and other closing arguments were not improper. There was sufficient evidence to submit kidnapping to the jury. The pecuniary gain aggravator was supported by taking a purse; the "in course of a kidnapping" aggravator by taking a car. There was no double counting. Since defendant's prior crimes could be construed as not violent, they had to be submitted as mitigators, although he argued they enforced the prior crime aggravator.


OUTCOME: The highest court affirmed the convictions, and, after conducting its independent proportionality review, concluded the case was more similar to cases in which it had found the sentence of death proportionate than disproportionate, and affirmed the death sentence.

State v. Tirado


No. 5A01


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


358 N.C. 551; 599 S.E.2d 515; 2004 N.C. LEXIS 911


February 3, 2003, Heard in the Supreme Court  
August 13, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected August 31, 2004.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Queen v. N.C., 544 U.S. 909, 125 S. Ct. 1600, 161 L. Ed. 2d 285, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 2312 (U.S., Mar. 7, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Tirado, 358 N.C. 738, 602 S.E.2d 680, 2004 N.C. LEXIS 947 (2004)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] DEFENDANT TIRADO: NO ERROR GUILT-INNOCENCE PHASE; DEATH SENTENCE VACATED; REMANDED FOR NEW CAPITAL SENTENCING PROCEEDING. DEFENDANT QUEEN: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were convicted by a jury in the Superior Court, Cumberland County (North Carolina), of first-degree murder, kidnapping, robbery with a dangerous weapon, conspiracy to commit first-degree murder, conspiracy to commit kidnapping, conspiracy to commit robbery with a dangerous weapon, and assault with a deadly weapon. The trial court imposed death sentences and prison terms to each defendant. Defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and a co-defendant were gang members. Three of the gang members, in carrying out an assignment, abducted a woman, put her in the trunk of her car, robbed her, and returned in the car to their leader's home. Defendant and other members then drove the woman to a remote area where one gang member shot her. However, the woman feigned death and survived her wounds. Because there was not enough room in the woman's car for all the gang members, defendant and other gang members then followed and stopped two women in their car. The women were placed in the trunk of the car, robbed, and driven to the leader's home. The gang members then drove the two cars to a secluded area where defendant shot and killed one woman and the co-defendant shot and killed the other woman. On appeal, the court found that (1) there was no error in the joint trial; (2) there was no error in the selection of jurors; and (3) there was no prejudicial error in the instructions. However, the trial court erred in denying the co-defendant's motion for mistrial because the jury poll was not timely and because intervening evidence the jury heard led to substantial and irreparable prejudice to the co-defendant.


OUTCOME: The convictions were affirmed; defendant's death sentence was affirmed; and the co-defendant's death sentence was vacated with a remand for a new capital sentencing proceeding.

 

78 of 825 DOCUMENTS



State v. Garcia


No. 504A01


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


358 N.C. 382; 597 S.E.2d 724; 2004 N.C. LEXIS 669



June 25, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Garcia v. North Carolina, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 1595 (U.S., Feb. 22, 2005)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed as of right from a judgment of the Superior Court, Wake County (North Carolina), which found defendant guilty of first-degree murder under the felony murder rule, with attempted rape as the underlying felony, and imposed a death sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant took the victim, at gunpoint, into the ladies' restroom of their apartment complex clubhouse, forced her to undress, and fatally beat her with his revolver. Defendant was convicted and sentenced, and he sought review. The court, pursuant to review under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a), found that denial of his motions to dismiss and for a bill of particulars was proper, as short-form indictments for homicide were authorized, and defendant was provided with open file discovery. Denial of suppression as to defendant's inculpatory statement was proper because defendant was not in custody, and errors in jury selection were not grounds for reversal. There was sufficient evidence of attempted rape to present to the jury, and admission of his statement to a holding cell officer was proper, as it was relevant. There was evidence of defendant's remorse, such that exclusion of other remorse evidence was harmless, and prosecutors' closing arguments were reasonable inferences based on the evidence. There was sufficient evidence to support the aggravating circumstance, and pursuant to the proportionality review, the sentence was not deemed excessive or disproportionate to others.


OUTCOME: The court found no error.


 

79 of 825 DOCUMENTS



State v. Roache


No. 522A01


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


358 N.C. 243; 595 S.E.2d 381; 2004 N.C. LEXIS 340


September 9, 2003, Heard in the Supreme Court  
May 7, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Roache, 555 S.E.2d 918, 2001 N.C. LEXIS 1072 (N.C., 2001)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed as of right, pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a), from judgments imposing sentences of death entered in Superior Court, Haywood County (North Carolina), upon a jury verdict finding him guilty of two counts of first-degree murder. The supreme court allowed defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to his appeal of additional judgments.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's many claims on appeal related to jury selection, the guilt-innocence phase, the sentencing proceeding, preservation issues, and proportionality. The supreme court found the trial court did not abuse its discretion in excusing two prospective jurors for cause, as both prospective jurors clearly demonstrated their inability to render a verdict in accordance with the state's laws. Defendant had shown no prejudice flowing from the fact that the State proceeded on two theories with regard to felony murder, namely "aiding and abetting" in connection with premeditation and deliberation, and "acting in concert." The State properly used victim impact testimony to describe the specific harm caused by defendant's actions. Defendant demonstrated no compelling reason to depart from the supreme court's prior holdings in regard to preservation issues. After a review of the transcript, record on appeal, briefs, and oral arguments of counsel, the jury's finding of three distinct aggravating circumstances submitted was supported by the evidence, and nothing suggested that defendant's death sentences were imposed under the influence of passion, prejudice, or any other arbitrary factor.


OUTCOME: The supreme court found no error.


 

80 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Jones


No. 22A02


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


358 N.C. 330; 595 S.E.2d 124; 2004 N.C. LEXIS 341


April 7, 2003, Heard in the Supreme Court  
May 7, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Jones v. North Carolina, 543 U.S. 1023, 125 S. Ct. 659, 160 L. Ed. 2d 500, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 8022 (2004)
Motion granted by State v. Jones, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 1000 (N.C., Dec. 9, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Jones, 570 S.E.2d 888, 2002 N.C. LEXIS 959 (N.C., 2002)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury sitting in the Superior Court, Onslow County (North Carolina), convicted defendant of murdering his wife and stepson. The jury recommended a sentence of death for each of the murders, and the trial court entered judgments convicting defendant and sentencing him to death. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with two counts of first-degree murder after police responded to his call for assistance and found that his wife, stepson, and defendant had been shot with a shotgun. The trial court allowed the State to introduce statements defendant made to nurses who treated him while he was in the hospital and also admitted an audiotape of an argument defendant had with his wife and stepson which his wife's relatives found 10 months after the wife and stepson were killed. The state supreme court held, inter alia, that (1) the trial court did not err when it granted the State's challenges for cause against two potential jurors who said they would have difficulty voting in favor of the death penalty; (2) the trial court did not err when it admitted the nurses' testimony; (3) the audiotape was properly authenticated, and it was properly admitted for the limited purpose of showing defendant's malice, intent, and ill will towards the victims; (4) the evidence supported the jury's finding that the murders were especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel; and (5) the sentence of death which was imposed on defendant was not disproportionate to the penalty imposed in similar cases.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court affirmed the trial court's judgments convicting defendant and sentencing him to death.
