Stephenson v. Bartlett


No. 94PA02-3


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


358 N.C. 219; 595 S.E.2d 112; 2004 N.C. LEXIS 312


March 18, 2004, Heard In the Supreme Court  
April 22, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion denied by Stephenson v. Bartlett, 359 N.C. 286, 610 S.E.2d 715, 2005 N.C. LEXIS 86 (2005)
Costs and fees proceeding at Stephenson v. Bartlett, 177 N.C. App. 239, 628 S.E.2d 442, 2006 N.C. App. LEXIS 870 (2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Johnston , No. 24A04 - Wake.
Stephenson v. Bartlett, 358 N.C. 149, 594 S.E.2d 24, 2004 N.C. LEXIS 183 (2004)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs and defendants in two consolidated actions appealed from an order of the Superior Court, Wake County (North Carolina), which transferred venue from one county to another, and which granted summary judgment to one set of party defendants. The action arose out of challenges to redistricting for House and Senate legislative elections.


OVERVIEW: In the first action, plaintiffs-A alleged that 2001 redistricting plans for the North Carolina House and Senate were flawed. The plan was found violative of N.C. Const. art. II, §§ 3, 5, and a permanent injunction was issued against future legislative elections. 2002 redistricting plans were also unconstitutional, but interim plans were upheld on appeal. Thereafter, a new redistricting plan for 2003 was enacted, and plaintiffs-B sought a determination as to the constitutionality thereof. Plaintiffs-A alleged that the 2003 plans were unconstitutional. Parts of the 2003 plan were found unconstitutional, with the remaining parts of the plan upheld. The court found that as action-A was complete, a challenge to the 2003 plans therein was error. The trial court properly denied a motion to dismiss by defendants-B, as plaintiffs-B had standing and a declaratory judgment action was proper. The three-judge panel of superior court judges required by N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-267.1 was proper, the venue provisions and assignment of judges under N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 1-81.1 and 1-267.1 were constitutional, and N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 120-2.3 and -2.4 were not unconstitutional limits on the judicial branch.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court finding the session law constitutional as modified.

 

82 of 825 DOCUMENTS



Rhyne v. K-Mart Corp.


No. 522A02


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


358 N.C. 160; 594 S.E.2d 1; 2004 N.C. LEXIS 193


September 9, 2003, Heard in the Supreme Court  
April 2, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Rhyne v. K-Mart Corp., 149 N.C. App. 672, 562 S.E.2d 82, 2002 N.C. App. LEXIS 316 (2002)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs appealed a decision of the Court of Appeals (North Carolina) that affirmed a superior court's order, which, after a jury, inter alia, awarded punitive damages to plaintiffs, reduced the punitive damages for both plaintiffs to comply with the statutory cap in N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1D-25. Plaintiffs claimed § 1D-25 was unconstitutional, and defendant store cross-appealed, contending the cap applied per defendant, not per plaintiff.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs sought compensatory and punitive damages based on several different claims for injuries they sustained during an incident with the store's security employees. Affirming the reduction of the punitive damages awards, the court held that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1D-25 was constitutional because, inter alia, (1) it did not represent an impermissible interference under N.C. Const. art. I, § 6 with the judiciary's constitutionally defined authority because it was a modification of the common law; (2) it did not deprive plaintiffs of their right to a jury trial under N.C. Const. art. 1, § 25 because they had no "property interest" to an award of punitive damages; (3) the limitation bore some rational relationship to several legitimate governmental interests, and, thus, did not violate principles due process and equal protection under N.C. Const. art. I, § 19; and (4) it did not violate the Open Courts Clause of N.C. Const. art. I, § 18, it was not vague, and it did not deprive plaintiffs of the "fruit of their labor." In addition, the trial court properly reduced the awards per plaintiff, rather than per defendant, under the court's construction of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1D-25.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's decision.


 

83 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Webb


No. 157PA03


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


358 N.C. 92; 591 S.E.2d 505; 2004 N.C. LEXIS 5


September 11, 2003, Heard In the Supreme Court  
February 6, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Webb, 584 S.E.2d 297, 2003 N.C. LEXIS 823 (N.C., 2003)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED AS MODIFIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On writ of certiorari, the State appealed from an order entered by the Superior Court, Durham County (North Carolina), declaring N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-455.1 (Supp. 2002) unconstitutional and enjoining the court clerk from collecting the appointment fee and entering civil judgments pursuant to § 7A-455.1(b). The SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA had issued a writ of supersedeas staying enforcement of the trial court's order.


OVERVIEW: The appointment fee was assessed against indigent defendants who received appointed counsel regardless of the outcome of the criminal proceedings. The State's highest court held that the portion of the appointment fee that was allocated to the North Carolina Indigent Persons' Attorney Fee Fund was a cost of prosecution. The portion allocated to the North Carolina Court Information Technology Fund was a facilities fee. Under N.C. Const. art. I, § 23, the appointment fee could not be imposed until defendant was convicted or pled guilty or nolo contendere. However, 2002 N.C. Sess. Laws 126, which enacted N.C. Gen. Stat. ch. 7A, had a severability clause. Thus, the requirement that payment be made regardless of the outcome could be severed to allow the State to assess the fee against convicted defendants. The portion of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-455.1(a) (Supp. 2002) requiring payment "at the time of appointment" was also severed, as was § 7A-455.1(b). The remaining provisions of § 7A-455.1 could be enforced independently. As modified, § 7A-455.1 did not have an unconstitutional chilling effect on a defendant's constitutional rights to counsel and notice.


OUTCOME: The trial court's decision was affirmed, as modified.

 

84 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Mitchell


No. 655PA02


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


358 N.C. 63; 592 S.E.2d 543; 2004 N.C. LEXIS 17


October 16, 2003, Heard in the Supreme Court  
February 6, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Mitchell, 154 N.C. App. 186, 571 S.E.2d 640, 2002 N.C. App. LEXIS 1414 (2002)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] MODIFIED AND AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with driving while impaired. Defendant was found guilty in the district court, but on appeal to the superior court, the trial court granted defendant's motion to suppress. The Court of Appeals (North Carolina) reversed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The police had set up a road checkpoint. Defendant did not stop at the checkpoint, but drove through it, nearly striking the officer. The officer pursued defendant, who finally stopped more than a mile beyond the checkpoint. Defendant argued that the checkpoint was unconstitutionally authorized. The intermediate appellate court ruled that the trial court erred by analyzing the stop and arrest in terms of the legality of the checkpoint. The appellate court held that the relevant issue was whether the officer had reasonable articulable suspicion to stop defendant after he drove through the checkpoint. The officer had reasonable articulable suspicion that defendant committed several crimes. The checkpoint stop was a minimal intrusion. The officer's standing permission to set up checkpoints pursuant oral guidelines and the officer's call to his supervisor before setting up the checkpoint were constitutionally sufficient restraints. The need for written guidelines or supervisory permission were not lynchpins for the constitutionality of a checkpoint. Defendant's flight and the surrounding circumstances gave the officer reasonable articulable suspicion to stop defendant.


OUTCOME: The appellate court modified the opinion of the intermediate appellate court by concluding that defendant's stop and arrest was proper without regard to whether the checkpoint was constitutional. As modified, the judgment of the intermediate appellate court was affirmed.

State v. Squires


No. 428A00


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


357 N.C. 529; 591 S.E.2d 837; 2003 N.C. LEXIS 1265


May 6, 2003, Heard in the Supreme Court  
November 7, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Squires v. North Carolina, 541 U.S. 1088, 159 L. Ed. 2d 252, 124 S. Ct. 2818, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 4087 (U.S., June 7, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Squires, 357 N.C. 469, 584 S.E.2d 81, 2003 N.C. LEXIS 764 (2003)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Superior Court, Pitt County (North Carolina), of two counts of first-degree murder. The jury recommended that defendant be sentenced to life imprisonment without parole for one murder and to death for the other murder. The trial court imposed the sentences. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two first-degree murders arising out of a drug transaction -- one on the basis of premeditation and deliberation and under the felony murder rule, for which he did not receive the death penalty, and one solely under the felony murder rule, for which he did receive the death penalty. On appeal, the court found that (1) the evidence was sufficient for a reasonable juror to find attempted sale of cocaine by defendant, a lesser-included offense of sale of cocaine, so the trial court's submission to the jury of sale of cocaine as a predicate felony to support defendant's felony murder conviction for one victim's death was not error; (2) the trial court did not err by trying defendant under the short form bills of indictment issued in the case; (3) the murder of the other victim was properly used to support submission of the N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(e)(11) (2001) aggravating circumstance to the jury; (4) the trial court's submission of the N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(e)(3) aggravating circumstance to the jury was not error; and (5) the trial court correctly denied defendant's request to submit his prior sentences as a mitigating circumstance.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Stephenson v. Bartlett


No. 94PA02-2


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


357 N.C. 301; 582 S.E.2d 247; 2003 N.C. LEXIS 745


March 10, 2003, Heard in the Supreme Court  
July 16, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Stephenson v. Bartlett, 357 N.C. 470, 587 S.E.2d 342, 2003 N.C. LEXIS 1069 (2003)
Related proceeding at United States v. Black, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 41295 (E.D.N.C., June 5, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: Stephenson v. Bartlett, 355 N.C. 757, 565 S.E.2d 188, 2002 N.C. LEXIS 626 (2002)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs voter, elected officials, and political party chairman sued defendants election board director and members, house speaker, senate president, governor, and attorney general in the Superior Court, Johnston County (North Carolina), alleging a redistricting plan was unconstitutional. The trial court agreed, the legislature submitted a new plan, and the trial court found that plan unconstitutional, adopted a plan, and defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court held the evidence supported the trial court's findings of fact that the legislature's new redistricting plan was constitutionally deficient in numerous instances. These findings of fact adequately supported the trial court's conclusion that the new redistricting plans failed to attain strict compliance with the legal requirements previously set forth by the supreme court and were unconstitutional. The new plan involved excessive division of counties, contrary to N.C. Const. art. II, §§ 3(3) and 5(3), deficiencies in county groupings, and substantial failures in compactness, contiguity, and communities of interest. The revised senate plan cut across interior county boundaries more than necessary, clustered portions of counties to structure individual county groups, and violated the requirement that districts be compact. The revised house plan cut county lines more than necessary to comply with the federal "one-person, one-vote" requirement and the Voting Rights Act of 1965, 42 U.S.C.S. § 1971, and violated the mandate that districts be compact.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.

State v. Hunt


No. 5A86-8


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


357 N.C. 257; 582 S.E.2d 593; 2003 N.C. LEXIS 746


April 8, 2003, Heard in the Supreme Court  
July 16, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Stay denied by, US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Hunt v. North Carolina, 156 L. Ed. 2d 702, 124 S. Ct. 44, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 5420 (U.S., Sept. 11, 2003)
Stay denied by, Petition denied by Hunt v. North Carolina, 156 L. Ed. 2d 702, 124 S. Ct. 43, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 5421 (U.S., Sept. 11, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: Hunt v. Lee, 291 F.3d 284, 2002 U.S. App. LEXIS 9760 (4th Cir. N.C., 2002)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate, convicted of 2 capital murders over 17 years ago, filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus challenging the lawfulness of the charging instruments, known as short-form indictment, used to indict him for first-degree murder. The Superior Court, Robeson County (North Carolina), denied the inmate's petition. The inmate was granted certiorari review.


OVERVIEW: The issue before the supreme court was whether the United State's Supreme Court's decision in Ring v. Arizona rendered North Carolina's short-form murder indictment unconstitutional. The supreme court held that Ring did not require state-court murder indictments to allege the aggravating circumstances to be presented against capital defendants. The supreme court concluded that the Fifth Amendment did not apply to state prosecutions. The supreme court noted that only possible constitutional implication that Ring and Apprendi could have had in relation to North Carolina capital defendants was that they must have received reasonable notice of aggravating circumstances, pursuant to the Sixth Amendment's notice requirement.


OUTCOME: The trial court's denial of the inmate's petition for habeas relief was affirmed.

 

88 of 825 DOCUMENTS


In re Stumbo


No. 321A01


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


357 N.C. 279; 582 S.E.2d 255; 2003 N.C. LEXIS 747


February 11, 2002, Heard in the Supreme Court  
July 16, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: In re Stumbo, 143 N.C. App. 375, 547 S.E.2d 451, 2001 N.C. App. LEXIS 304 (2001)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the Cleveland County, N.C., Department of Social Services (agency), received an anonymous call of an unsupervised two-year-old child, naked in the driveway of a house. Respondent parents refused to allow a caseworker to privately interview the child or his siblings. The trial court ordered the parents not to interfere with the investigation, which was affirmed by the Court of Appeals (North Carolina). The parents appealed.


OVERVIEW: At the hearing before the trial court, the father attempted to explain what happened. Objections were sustained, and the trial court instructed the father to confine his testimony to the events that transpired at the time the caseworker arrived at his home. All further evidence related solely to the effort of the caseworker to interview the children in private, and the parents' refusal to allow her to do so. The highest appellate court found that it was never determined whether there was sufficient evidence of neglect to trigger the investigative requirements of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7B-302. An anonymous call, reporting a naked child, two years of age, unsupervised in a driveway, did not, standing alone, constitute a report of abuse, neglect, or dependency. There was no evidence of a pattern of a lack of supervision or other credible evidence that indicated a serious failing by the parents. Therefore, the investigative mandate of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7B-302 was not properly invoked. It followed that the trial court's order, based upon the petition filed pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7B-303, charging the parents with interference with or obstruction of an investigation, would have to fail.


OUTCOME: The decision of the lower appellate court was reversed, and the case was remanded to the lower appellate court for further remand to the trial court, for the entry of an order consistent with the instant opinion.

 

89 of 825 DOCUMENTS



Williams v. Blue Cross Blue Shield


No. 277PA01


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


357 N.C. 170; 581 S.E.2d 415; 2003 N.C. LEXIS 595; 92 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 404


December 11, 2001, Heard in the Supreme Court  
June 13, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Williams v. Blue Cross Blue Shield of N.C., 552 S.E.2d 637, 2001 N.C. LEXIS 703 (N.C., 2001)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee sued appellee employer in the Superior Court, Orange County (North Carolina), for age and sex discrimination and retaliatory firing. The employer counterclaimed for a declaratory judgment that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 160A-492 (2001) and Orange County, N.C., Civil Rights Ordinance art. II, § 2.1(a) (1995) violated N.C. Const. art. II, § 24(1)(j). The trial court granted summary judgment to the employer. The employee appealed.


OVERVIEW: The employee alleged that she was forced to resign from her employer because of her age and sex, and also that the employer retaliated against her for filing her discrimination claim. The employer counterclaimed that the employment provisions of the county ordinance, Orange County, N.C., Civil Rights Ordinance art. II, § 2.1(a), under which the employee brought suit and the enabling act for that ordinance, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 160A-492, were unconstitutional as they violated N.C. Const. art. II, § 24(1)(j). The supreme court found that the employer's counterclaim was brought well within any limitations period. Further, there was no unreasonable delay in bringing the challenge so that the counterclaim was not barred by laches. Finally, neither the enabling legislation nor the employment provisions of the ordinance suggested any rational basis for justifying the treatment of Orange County differently from all other North Carolina counties as to employment rights. By seeking to curb unlawful discrimination by regulating covered employers, the enabling legislation and the ordinance had the practical effect of regulating labor, which was forbidden by N.C. Const. art. II, § 24(1)(j).


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


 

90 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Spivey


No. 299A02


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


357 N.C. 114; 579 S.E.2d 251; 2003 N.C. LEXIS 422


March 11, 2003, Heard in the Supreme Court  
May 2, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Spivey, 150 N.C. App. 189, 563 S.E.2d 12, 2002 N.C. App. LEXIS 394 (2002)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA affirmed the trial court's judgment denying defendant's motion to dismiss for lack of a speedy trial and its entry of a final judgment after defendant pled guilty to second-degree murder while reserving the right to appeal the speedy trial issue. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant turned himself in and admitted he shot another man to death. The evidence indicated defendant and the victim had a conflict over a woman. Defendant filed a handwritten, pro se speedy trial motion one year later. Nearly 21 months later, defendant's court-appointed attorneys filed a motion to dismiss for lack of a speedy trial. At a hearing, the State stipulated to statements made by two potential witnesses. One witness was in prison. The other witness, the woman, could not be located, but the State informed the court it was trying to find her. The State also presented defendant with a list of the large number of murder cases tried between his indictment and the hearing. The motion was denied. Defendant pled guilty to second-degree murder right before trial and shortly after the woman had been located. After the appellate court affirmed the denial of the motion, the state supreme court found that application of the four-factor Barker test showed that defendant's right to a speedy trial had not been violated despite a four-year delay between his indictment and plea bargain, especially since the court's crowded docket and not the State's inaction contributed to the delay.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's judgment was affirmed.

State v. Walters


No. 548A00


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


357 N.C. 68; 588 S.E.2d 344; 2003 N.C. LEXIS 570


May 14, 2002, Heard in the Supreme Court  
May 2, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected May 22, 2003.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Walters v. North Carolina, 540 U.S. 971, 124 S. Ct. 442, 157 L. Ed. 2d 320, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 7795 (Oct. 20, 2003)
Subsequent appeal at, Motion granted by State v. Augustine, 367 N.C. 236, 740 S.E.2d 464, 2013 N.C. LEXIS 333 (Apr. 1, 2013)
Motion granted by State v. Walters, 2017 N.C. LEXIS 154 (N.C., Mar. 17, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Walters, 564 S.E.2d 232, 2002 N.C. LEXIS 487 (N.C., May 9, 2002)



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment by the Superior Court, Cumberland County (North Carolina) that, inter alia, convicted her of murder and sentenced her to death; defendant claimed, inter alia, that the trial court erred in admitting certain photographs of the victims and that the prosecutor's closing arguments were improper.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and other gang members decided that they needed money. They stole two different cars at different times, put the cars' drivers in the trunks, and drove to remote locations, where the victims were robbed and shot. Only one of the three victims survived. During the trial, photographs of the two murdered victims were admitted into evidence. Later, during closing arguments, one of the prosecutors allegedly compared defendant to Adolph Hitler. The state supreme court held, inter alia, that pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 8C-1, N.C. R. Evid. 403, the trial court properly exercised its discretion in admitting one photograph to identify a particular victim and to illustrate what a police officer observed. A second photograph was properly admitted to show the relative location of the victims. The closing argument consisted of a rambling, disjointed personal attack on defendant, filled with irrelevant historical references and name-calling. Although the argument was improper, given the overwhelming evidence of defendant's guilt, she was not prejudiced thereby.


OUTCOME: No error was found and the judgment was affirmed.

93 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Haselden


No. 665A01


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


357 N.C. 1; 577 S.E.2d 594; 2003 N.C. LEXIS 318


February 4, 2003, Heard In the Supreme Court  
March 28, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Haselden v. North Carolina, 540 U.S. 988, 124 S. Ct. 475, 157 L. Ed. 2d 382, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 8097 (U.S., Nov. 3, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Haselden, 565 S.E.2d 184, 2002 N.C. LEXIS 621 (N.C., 2002)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following a jury trial, the Superior Court, Stokes County (North Carolina), convicted defendant of first-degree murder and robbery with a firearm. The jury recommended that defendant be sentenced to death for murder, and the trial court imposed that sentence for the murder, and sentenced defendant to imprisonment for a minimum of 103 months and a maximum of 130 months for robbery. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder and robbery after he shot a female acquaintance in the face with a shotgun and took her car and other personal property. The state supreme court held that (1) altercations which defendant had before trial and threats he made against his attorney and others justified the trial court's decision to leave defendant in shackles during trial; (2) defendant did not have the right to receive written statements the prosecution's witnesses made until the witnesses testified, and the trial court properly denied defendant's motions to dismiss the charges and for a mistrial, based on a claim that the State failed to disclose exculpatory evidence; (3) the trial court did not abuse its discretion by admitting photographs of the victim that showed her wounds; (4) the prosecutor's use of Biblical references during his argument to the jury in favor of the death penalty were not so grossly improper that the trial court erred by failing to intervene ex mero motu; and (5) N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(e)(9) (2001) was constitutional, and the trial court did not err by asking the jury to consider whether the murder defendant committed was especially heinous or cruel.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court affirmed the trial court's judgments convicting defendant of first-degree murder and robbery with a firearm, and sentencing defendant to death for the murder and imprisonment for the robbery.

 

94 of 825 DOCUMENTS


In re Greens of Pine Glen


No. 681PA01


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


356 N.C. 642; 576 S.E.2d 316; 2003 N.C. LEXIS 27


September 9, 2002, Heard in the Supreme Court  
February 28, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  In re Greens of Pine Glen Ltd. P'ship., 147 N.C. App. 221, 555 S.E.2d 612, 2001 N.C. App. LEXIS 1139 (2001).



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent taxpayer instituted an action against petitioner county to review the county's ad valorem tax valuation of the taxpayer's property. The state property tax commission confirmed the valuation assigned by the county. The Court of Appeals (North Carolina) reversed. The county appealed.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer built a low income housing project pursuant to 26 U.S.C.S. § 42 (2000), which provided income tax credits for such construction. The county valued the property using the cost approach. The taxpayer wanted the property to be valued by the income approach, taking into account the market impact of 26 U.S.C.S. § 42 (2000) use and rent restrictions. The appellate court ruled that a taxpayer challenging an ad valorem tax assessment needed to show that the means used by the tax supervisor were illegal or arbitrary and also that the valuation was unreasonably high. The taxpayer failed to show that the assessed value of the property exceeded its fair market value. N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-317 allowed the cost approach. Where the income approach was used, the valuation was to be based on market rents, not contractually restricted rents. Because 26 U.S.C.S. § 42 (2000) restrictions were freely entered contractual covenants, not governmental regulations, a taxpayer was not allowed to artificially alter the value of its property below fair market value. Since the evidence was known before the hearing, the tax commission considered the incorrect square footage of the property.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed. The case was remanded to the property tax commission for the limited purpose of substituting in its final decision the correct square footage of the property.

 

95 of 825 DOCUMENTS


Augur v. Augur


No. 218A02


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


356 N.C. 582; 573 S.E.2d 125; 2002 N.C. LEXIS 1262


October 16, 2002, Heard In the Supreme Court  
December 20, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal pursuant to N. C.G.S. § 7A-30(2) from the decision of a divided panel of the Court of Appeals, 149 N.C. App. 851, 561 S.E.2d 568 (2002), affirming in part and reversing and remanding in part a judgment entered 11 December 2000 by Cash, J., in District Court, Buncombe County.



DISPOSITION: COURT OF APPEALS REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a wife, moved for a domestic violence protection order (DVPO) against defendant, her husband. The trial court dismissed the complaint and denied the husband's request for a declaratory judgment on the constitutionality of the North Carolina Domestic Violence Act (DVA), N.C. Gen. Stat. ch. 50B (2001). The husband appealed, and the North Carolina Court of Appeals reversed and remanded in part. The wife appealed the judgment.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court found that an actual controversy existed between the parties for purposes of the North Carolina Uniform Declaratory Judgment Act (NCUDJA), N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 1-253 to 1-267 (2001), and that the husband was entitled to a ruling on the constitutionality of the DVA. The state supreme court held that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-257 permitted a trial court, in the exercise of its discretion, to decline a request for declaratory relief when (1) the requested declaration would serve no useful purpose in clarifying or settling the legal relations at issue, or (2) the requested declaration would not terminate or afford relief from the uncertainty, insecurity, or controversy giving rise to the proceeding. The state supreme court held that in the instant case, the trial court properly declined the husband's request for declaratory relief. Because the husband was not subject to the provisions of the DVA at the time the trial court addressed his counterclaim and because he made no showing that he was threatened with further litigation under the DVA, a declaration as to the constitutionality of the DVA could not have altered the husband's legal position.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

State v. Wagner


No. 108A02


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


356 N.C. 599; 572 S.E.2d 777; 2002 N.C. LEXIS 1259


September 12, 2002, Heard In the Supreme Court  
December 20, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal pursuant to N. C.G.S. § 7A-30(2) from the decision of a divided panel of the Court of Appeals, 148 N.C. App. 658, 560 S.E.2d 174 (2002), finding no error in judgments entered 17 October 2000 by Albright, J., in Superior Court, Forsyth County.



DISPOSITION: DECISION OF COURT OF APPEALS VACATED IN PART AND REVERSED AND REMANDED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of attempted possession of cocaine, felonious possession of drug paraphernalia, and being a habitual felon. The North Carolina Court of Appeals affirmed the convictions. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court sentenced defendant to serve 101 to 131 months' confinement for attempted possession of cocaine. Defendant filed a motion for appropriate relief asserting that his record level had been improperly calculated as a Level VI when in fact his criminal history resulted in a Level V for sentencing purposes. The trial court vacated and set aside the guilty plea and the judgment. Defendant argued that the felony drug paraphernalia indictment after his successful motion for appropriate relief was in violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-1335 (2001) and that the subsequent sentence for attempted possession of cocaine also violated § 15A-1335. The supreme court held that the indictment for felonious possession of drug paraphernalia was invalid, as the charge was not supported by any statute. Thus, the appellate court lacked jurisdiction to hear defendant's appeal. After defendant's plea and sentence were set aside pursuant to his motion for appropriate relief, a sentence of 135 to 175 months' imprisonment for attempted possession of cocaine was contrary to § 15A-1335 when defendant's original sentence was only 101 to 131 months' imprisonment for the same offense.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for felonious possession of drug paraphernalia was vacated. The appellate court's judgment regarding defendant's conviction for attempted possession of cocaine, was reversed and remanded to that court for further remand to the trial court for resentencing.

State v. Carroll


No. 587A01


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


356 N.C. 526; 573 S.E.2d 899; 2002 N.C. LEXIS 1265


October 16, 2002, Heard In the Supreme Court  
December 20, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Carroll v. N.C., 2003 U.S. LEXIS 4928 (U.S., June 23, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N. C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Thompson, J., on 29 May 2001 in Superior Court, Cumberland County, upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder.
State v. Carroll, 567 S.E.2d 145, 2002 N.C. LEXIS 700 (N.C., 2002)



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the Superior Court in Cumberland County (North Carolina), which convicted him, on a jury verdict, of first-degree murder based on malice, premeditation and deliberation, and under the felony murder rule. Following a capital sentencing proceeding, the jury recommended a sentence of death, and the trial court entered judgment in accordance with that recommendation.


OVERVIEW: Defendant attacked his live-in girlfriend with a machete, carried her to the bedroom, and then killed her by strangulation. The court found that use of the assault as an underlying felony for the felony murder conviction was appropriate because the assault was a separate offense from the murder and did not merge into the homicide. Further, testimony about the victim's statements the day before the murder, to the effect that she was tired of defendant taking her money to buy drugs, and that she had asked him to leave, indicated difficulties in the relationship prior to the murder. Thus, the statements were admissible to show the victim's state of mind, pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 8C-1, N.C. R. Evid. 803(3). The court also found that a summary paragraph from a certified foreign judgment was properly admitted to support an aggravating circumstance under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(e)(3). A court clerk testified that the documents were signed and certified in a manner verifying their authenticity, and a fingerprint expert testified without objection that the fingerprints from the foreign jurisdiction were those of defendant. The court concluded that defendant received a fair trial.


OUTCOME: The court found no error and left undisturbed the judgment and sentence entered by the trial court.

State v. Barden

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N. C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Balog, J., on 12 November 1999 in Superior Court, Sampson County, upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder. On 25 June 2001, the Supreme Court allowed defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to an additional judgment.
State v. Barden, 550 S.E.2d 510, 2001 N.C. LEXIS 715 (N.C., 2001)



DISPOSITION: REMANDED FOR BATSON HEARING; OTHERWISE NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by a jury in the Superior Court, Sampson County (North Carolina), of first-degree murder under the felony murder rule and guilty of robbery with a dangerous weapon. The jury recommended a sentence of death. The trial court entered judgment imposing a death sentence for the murder conviction while arresting judgment on the armed robbery conviction. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: A police detective asked defendant to go to a police station for questioning about the murder of defendant's co-worker. Defendant was told that he was not under arrest and was free to leave. The detective asked for and received defendant's shoes because he thought he saw blood on the shoes. Days later, the detective again asked to speak with defendant about the murder at the station. Defendant again was told that he was not under arrest and was free to leave. Defendant gave a confession, left the station, and was arrested hours later. Defendant argued that his statements and the blood on his shoes, which belonged to the victim, were inadmissible because he was not read his Miranda rights before the questionings. On appeal, the supreme court found that the police were not required to give defendant Miranda warnings, as defendant was not in custody when he made the statements, and the statements were given voluntarily. Further, the blood on his shoes was admissible, as consent to search was given. However, the trial court's conclusion that defendant failed to present a prima facie showing to challenge the prosecutor's peremptory strikes of prospective minority jurors was in error.


OUTCOME: The case was remanded for the limited purpose of holding a Batson hearing; in all other respects, defendant received a fair trial and capital sentencing proceeding, free from prejudicial error.

State v. Prevatte


No. 492A99


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


356 N.C. 178; 570 S.E.2d 440; 2002 N.C. LEXIS 942


March 11, 2002, Heard In the Supreme Court  
October 4, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion denied by State v. Prevatte, 2003 N.C. LEXIS 60 (N.C., Feb. 11, 2003)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Prevatte v. N.C., 2003 U.S. LEXIS 3085 (U.S., Apr. 21, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N. C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Helms, J., on 22 February 1999 in Superior Court, Stanly County, upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder. On 19 July 2001, the Supreme Court allowed defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to his appeal of additional judgments.
State v. Prevatte, 570 S.E.2d 109, 2002 N.C. LEXIS 901 (N.C., 2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court, Stanly County (North Carolina), in a second jury trial, convicted defendant of first-degree murder (on theories of malice, premeditation, and deliberation, and the felony murder rule) and two counts of second-degree kidnapping (of the victim, who was his girlfriend but who was reuniting with her husband, and her son) and imposed the death sentence. Defendant appealed as of right pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant entered the victim's home, took her and her son to the bedroom, made them get on their knees, hit and kicked her, pointed the gun at the son's head and said if she did not shut up, he would shoot the son, brought her out of the house, with her hands bound behind her back, forced her into a car and back out of the car, struck her three to four times and slammed her head into the car, and shot her more than once when she tried to run away. The high court, in affirming, found the trial court did not abuse its discretion by changing venue to Stanly County; defendant had requested a change and the State had consented, and the trial court had inherent power to change venue in the interests of justice notwithstanding the apparent statutory limitation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-957 (2001). Defendant did not have a right to replace his counsel because he was dissatisfied with their tactics. Prosecutorial comments were reviewed and were not misconduct. A statement that the victim and her husband were reuniting was admitted under a hearsay exception. The supreme court exhaustively reviewed diminished capacity, insanity, aggravators, and statutory and nonstatutory mitigators.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that defendant received a fair trial and capital sentencing proceeding, free from prejudicial error, that, after its proportionality review, the sentence of death was neither disproportionate nor excessive based upon the characteristics of defendant and the crimes committed, and affirmed the judgments and sentences, including the death sentence for first-degree murder.


 

100 of 825 DOCUMENTS


State v. Leeper


No. 256A00


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


356 N.C. 55; 565 S.E.2d 1; 2002 N.C. LEXIS 551



June 28, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from judgments imposing sentences of death entered by Caldwell, J., on 22 February 2000 in Superior Court, Mecklenburg County, upon jury verdicts finding defendant guilt counts of first-degree murder. On 30 May 2001, the Supreme Court allowed defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to his appeal of additional judgments. Heard in the Supreme Court 11 February 2002.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of two first-degree murders on the basis of premeditation and deliberation and under the felony murder rule, and of conspiracy to commit robbery with a dangerous weapon, robbery with a dangerous weapon, and attempted robbery with a dangerous weapon. On the jury's recommendation, the Superior Court, Mecklenburg County (North Carolina), sentenced him to death for each murder conviction. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: On the same day, defendant fatally shot two men in two separate robberies. He confessed to both murders. He claimed error in a witness's reference to his prior experience with Miranda warnings. The supreme court held that, as he acknowledged shooting both victims, any error was harmless. The amended version of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(e)(3) was unambiguous, and allowed consideration of his juvenile adjudication for what would have been felony armed robbery had he been an adult. A jury charge did not allow jurors to consider his juvenile crime as evidence to support the "course of conduct" aggravating circumstance. Use of the juvenile conviction did not implicate the ex post facto prohibition. His claims of error on six issues to permit the supreme court to reexamine its prior holdings and preserve them for review were rejected. The record supported the aggravating circumstances the jury found, and the death sentence was not imposed under the influence of passion, prejudice, or other arbitrary factors. Finally, in light of the three statutory aggravators the jury found, and the egregious disregard for human life defendant showed, the death sentence was not disproportionate.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Williams

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N. C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from judgments imposing sentences of death entered by Farmer, J., on 4 March 1998 in Superior Court, Wake County, upon jury verdicts finding defendant guilty of two counts of first-degree murder. DISPOSITION: NO. 97CRS8388, NO. 97CRS17582, FIRST-DEGREE MURDER: NO ERROR. NO. 97CRS17583, NO. 97CRS17584, FIRST-DEGREE RAPE: REMANDED FOR RESENTENCING. NO. 97CRS17587, FIRST-DEGREE SEXUAL OFFENSE: REMANDED FOR RESENTENCING. NO. 97CRS17588, ASSAULT WITH A DEADLY WEAPON: REMANDED FOR RESENTENCING. NO. 97CRS17590, NO. 97CRS17591, ASSAULT WITH A DEADLY WEAPON WITH INTENT TO KILL INFLICTING SERIOUS INJURY: REMANDED FOR RESENTENCING. PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the North Carolina trial court of two counts of first-degree murder, first-degree rape, first-degree sexual offense, assault with a deadly weapon, assault with a deadly weapon with intent to kill inflicting serious injury, attempted first-degree rape, and assault with a deadly weapon with intent to kill. The trial court sentenced defendant to death and to prison terms at a prior record Level V. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sexually assaulted various women in a neighborhood over time. The victims and the assaults fit a similar pattern. Two of the women were strangled to death in the assaults. DNA evidence led to defendant's convictions. On appeal, the court found that (1) the trial court did not abuse its discretion in joining the offenses for trial; (2) the trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion for a continuance; (3) the trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion for a bill of particulars; (4) the trial court did not abuse his discretion in denying defendant's request to receive state-funded expert assistance; (5) the trial court did not err in denying defendant's motions to suppress a photographic lineup identification and in-court identification; (6) the trial court correctly determined that peremptory challenges were not based solely upon race; (7) the trial court did not err in denying defendant's request for an in camera inspection; and (8) the photographs and videotape were not so gruesome and repetitive as to require their inadmissibility. However, the trial court erred in sentencing defendant at a prior record Level VI for the noncapital felonies.


OUTCOME: The convictions for the two murders and all the noncapital felonies were affirmed; the death sentences for the two murders were affirmed; however, the sentences for the noncapital felonies were reversed and the case was remanded for resentencing on the noncapital felony convictions at a prior record Level V.

Malloy v. Cooper


No. 595PA01


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


356 N.C. 113; 565 S.E.2d 76; 2002 N.C. LEXIS 543



June 28, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at, Remanded by Malloy v. Cooper, 2004 N.C. App. LEXIS 189 (N.C. Ct. App., Feb. 3, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On writ of certiorari pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-32(b) of a unanimous decision of the Court of Appeals, 146 N.C. App. 66, 551 S.E.2d 911 (2001), reversing an order entered 9 May 2000 by Spencer, J., in Superior Court, Granville County. Heard in the Supreme Court 15 April 2002.
Malloy v. Cooper, 146 N.C. App. 66, 551 S.E.2d 911, 2001 N.C. App. LEXIS 787 (2001)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant individual sued appellee state officials in the trial court for declaratory judgment as to the constitutionality of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-360. The trial court permanently enjoined the state officials from enforcing a portion of the statute. The COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA held that the trial court lacked jurisdiction to hear the suit and should have dismissed the action. The individual filed a petition for a writ of certiorari.


OVERVIEW: Twice a year the individual sponsored a pigeon shoot on his private land. He sponsored, organized, and operated the pigeon shoots for 12 years. People paid a fee to participate in the pigeon shoot. The individual spent $ 500,000 in capital improvements to his land to further the pigeon shoots and further claimed that the pigeon shoots provided approximately 50 percent of his net income. A district attorney notified the individual that the pigeon shoot violated N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-360, cruelty to animals, and that if the individual conducted the pigeon shoot, the individual would be prosecuted. The appellate court held that the individual's action should have been dismissed, pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1A-1, N.C. R. Civ. P. 12(b)(1). On appeal, the supreme court found that the case presented an actual controversy between parties with adverse interests. Furthermore, individual sufficiently alleged imminent prosecution and that he stood to lose fundamental human rights and property interests if the criminal statute was enforced and was later determined to be unconstitutional. Accordingly, the trial court properly denied the state officials' motion to dismiss.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court was reversed, and the case was remanded to the appellate court for a decision on the merits of the underlying action.

State v. Holmes

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal as of right pursuant to N. C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Thompson, J., on 8 September 2000 in Superior Court, Johnston County, upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder. On 31 July 2001, the Supreme Court allowed defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to his appeal of an additional judgment.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by a jury in the Johnston County Superior Court (North Carolina) of first-degree murder, attempted first-degree murder, and of robbery with a firearm. Defendant was sentenced to death for the murder conviction, to imprisonment for the attempted first-degree murder conviction, and to an arrested judgment on the robbery with a firearm conviction. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and two other men broke into a home to steal marijuana. Defendant shot and killed the owner of the home with a rifle. He shot another man who lied on the floor and pretended to be dead until defendant and the other men with him left. On appeal, the court found that: (1) the trial court acted within its discretion in ordering defendant to be restrained during the trial; (2) as the jury already determined beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant fired the rifle and earlier heard similar testimony, no reasonable probability existed that a detective's double-hearsay statement another man stated defendant said he shot the men affected the outcome of the sentencing proceeding; (3) the trial court properly permitted an expert witness to testify as to the consistency of the weapon used, the cartridges found, and the wounds inflicted; (4) the trial court properly amended a requested mitigator to avoid a misinterpretation unsupported by substantial evidence; (5) the trial court properly refused to submit the N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(f)(6) (2001) mitigating circumstance; and (6) defendant received a fair trial, and the sentence was proportionate.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Wiley


No. 100A01


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


355 N.C. 592; 565 S.E.2d 22; 2002 N.C. LEXIS 552


February 12, 2002, Heard in the Supreme Court  
June 28, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied: Wiley v. North Carolina, 154 L. Ed. 2d 795, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 24 (U.S. 2003).


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N. C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Lanier, J., on 27 May 1999 in Superior Court, New Hanover County, upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder. On 10 May 2001, the Supreme Court allowed defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to his appeal of additional judgments.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by a jury in the Superior Court, New Hanover County (North Carolina), of first-degree murder, first-degree kidnapping, and robbery with a dangerous weapon. The jury recommended a sentence of death for the first-degree murder conviction. The trial court entered judgment in accordance with that recommendation. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant hit the victim with a bottle. Another man and teenage girl then tied up the victim. They placed the victim in the trunk of his car and drove him to a secluded area. When the victim was released from the trunk, he attempted to flee, but defendant and the other man shot and killed him. On appeal, the supreme court found that: (1) defendant did not hold a subjective expectation of privacy in an unsealed envelope he delivered to a prison deputy, and if he did, this expectation was not objectively reasonable; (2) the trial court did not err in excusing a juror for cause, because of the juror's death penalty views; (3) the trial court did not err by limiting defendant's questioning of prospective jurors; (4) defendant was not denied effective assistance of counsel by counsel's admissions; (5) the State did not improperly vouch for witnesses; (6) there was no error in using defendant's juvenile adjudication as an aggravating circumstance; (7) defendant could prove no prejudice from an unrecorded charge conference; (8) the trial court did not err in failing to interrupt the State's closing argument; and (9) the sentence was proportional.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Hyatt


No. 402A00


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


355 N.C. 642; 566 S.E.2d 61; 2002 N.C. LEXIS 676



June 28, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied: Hyatt v. North Carolina, 154 L. Ed. 2d 823, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 390 (U.S. 2003).


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N. C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from judgments imposing sentences of death entered by Downs, J., on 4 February 2000 in Superior Court, Buncombe County, upon jury murder. On 29 October 2001, the Supreme Court allowed defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to his appeal of additional judgments. Heard in the Supreme Court 15 April 2002.
State v. Hyatt, 555 S.E.2d 922, 2001 N.C. LEXIS 1195 (N.C., 2001)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Superior Court, Buncombe County (North Carolina), of two counts each of first-degree kidnapping, robbery with a dangerous weapon, first-degree rape, and first-degree murder. He was sentenced to death and appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant kidnapped, robbed, raped and murdered two victims. The supreme court held his whispered request for counsel, not overheard by police, and stating his father wanted him to have counsel, did not invoke his right to counsel. Counsel's request to see defendant was also insufficient. A common modus operandi and temporal proximity allowed the murders' trial joinder. Evidence of defendant's premeditation and deliberation, countered only by his denial, did not allow a second-degree murder instruction. Defendant's kidnap and robbery victim could testify under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 8C-1, N.C. R. Evid. 404(b). Testimony about a rape kit used when the crimes occurred but since lost was proper as it was unlikely defendant could have tested it, and no willful intent to lose it was shown. A prosecution witness with speech problems was competent. A victim's fear of defendant's knife proved lack of consent. Arrest of judgment on the felonies underlying defendant's felony murder convictions was not required due to his conviction based on premeditation and deliberation. Defendant could not substitute retained counsel for appointed counsel during trial. His death sentence was proportional.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction and sentence were affirmed.

State v. Rogers


No. 373A00


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


355 N.C. 420; 562 S.E.2d 859; 2002 N.C. LEXIS 429


February 13, 2002, Heard In the Supreme Court  
May 10, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by State v. Rogers, 629 S.E.2d 283, 2006 N.C. LEXIS 534 (N.C., 2006)
Writ denied by State v. Rogers, 629 S.E.2d 283, 2006 N.C. LEXIS 531 (N.C., 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Haigwood, J., on 14 April 2000 in Superior Court, Halifax County, upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder. On 21 June 2001, the Supreme Court allowed defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to his appeal of additional judgments.
State v. Rogers, 551 S.E.2d 433, 2001 N.C. LEXIS 714 (N.C., 2001)



DISPOSITION: NO. 97CRS6891, FIRST-DEGREE MURDER: No error in the trial; death sentence vacated and case remanded for new capital sentencing proceeding. NO. 97CRS6892, FIRST-DEGREE BURGLARY: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Superior Court, Halifax County (North Carolina), convicted defendant of first-degree murder, first degree burglary, and first degree sexual offense. The jury recommended the death penalty and the trial court sentenced defendant to, inter alia, death. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred in refusing to change venue due to pretrial publicity. The appellate court held that defendant failed to show the specific and identifiable prejudice against him as a result of pretrial publicity needed to support a change of venue. The trial court's actions ensured that jury voir dire was conducted properly. The trial court did not err in denying a challenge for cause of a prospective juror who had been rehabilitated following initial answers making her vulnerable to a cause challenge. There was no error in the denial of individualized jury selection. A photo array was not improperly suggestive. The burden of persuasion was on defendant in making a Batson motion, and the trial court did not err in denying that motion. Other jurors were properly excused on the basis of age. The prosecutor's closing argument pointing out defendant's failure to answer the State's evidence was not an improper comment on defendant's failure to testify. However, the cumulative effect of the improprieties in the prosecutor's cross-examination of defendant's expert and his closing argument unfairly prejudiced defendant in the penalty phase of the trial.


OUTCOME: The appellate court held that defendant's trial was free from prejudicial error. However, the appellate court held that defendant was entitled to a new capital sentencing proceeding.

Stephenson v. Bartlett


No. 94PA02


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


355 N.C. 354; 562 S.E.2d 377; 2002 N.C. LEXIS 420



April 30, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Stay denied by: Bartlett v. Stephenson, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 3788 (U.S. May 17, 2002).
Motion dismissed by Stephenson v. Bartlett, 355 N.C. 500, 564 S.E.2d 234, 2002 N.C. LEXIS 470 (2002)
Motion to strike denied by Stephenson v. Bartlett, 355 N.C. 500, 564 S.E.2d 234, 2002 N.C. LEXIS 471 (2002)
Stay denied by Bartlett v. Stephenson, 535 U.S. 1301, 122 S. Ct. 1751, 152 L. Ed. 2d 1015, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 3788 (2002)
Petition granted by Stephenson v. Bartlett, 355 N.C. 757, 565 S.E.2d 188, 2002 N.C. LEXIS 626 (2002)


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-31(b) prior to determination by the Court of Appeals from an order allowing summary judgment for plaintiffs and an order for declaratory judgment and injunctive relief holding that the 2001 State legislative redistricting plans violate the North Carolina Constitution, both orders entered 20 February 2002 by Jenkins, J., in Superior Court, Johnston County. Heard in Special Session in the Supreme Court 4 April 2002.
Stephenson v. Bartlett, 563 S.E.2d 187, 2002 N.C. LEXIS 520 (N.C., 2002)
Stephenson v. Bartlett, 180 F. Supp. 2d 779, 2001 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 23697 (E.D.N.C., 2001)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED AS MODIFIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, voters and others (voters), brought suit against defendants, state election board officials and others (board), alleging legislative redistricting plans violated the North Carolina Constitution. After federal removal and remand, the Superior Court, Johnston County (North Carolina), granted summary judgment to the voters. The board appealed, and expedited direct review was granted. A stay prohibiting plan implementation was entered.


OVERVIEW: The board, attempting to justify the redistricting plan, argued that the whole-county provisions (WCP) of N.C. Const. art. II, §§ 3(3), 5(3) were no longer enforceable, because of the Voting Rights Act of 1965 (VRA), 42 U.S.C.S. § 1973 et seq. In rejecting the board's argument, the highest court found that the VRA did not require a state to adopt any particular reapportionment plan, only that it not dilute the voting strength of legally protected minority groups. In interpreting the North Carolina Constitution, the intentions of the people, as stated therein, would be accorded the fullest effect possible. County boundary lines were not respected in the redistricting plan. The highest court concluded that the legislature improperly divided counties in creating legislative districts, beyond the extent necessary to comply with federal law. Since the redistricting plans were in violation of the WCP, they were unconstitutional. As for the appropriate remedy, strict scrutiny analysis was applied. The highest court set forth restrictions as to what would be permissible, in crossing county boundary lines, in the adoption of a new redistricting plan.


OUTCOME: The orders of the trial court were affirmed, as modified. The stay issued by the highest appellate court was lifted, and the trial court was authorized to enter such further orders as necessary to implement the holdings of the highest appellate court.

State v. Robinson


No. 578A00


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


355 N.C. 320; 561 S.E.2d 245; 2002 N.C. LEXIS 330


November 13, 2001, Heard In the Supreme Court  
April 5, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected April 11, 2002. Writ of certiorari denied: Robinson v. North Carolina, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 8164 (U.S. Nov. 4, 2002).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal as of right pursuant to N. C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Brown (Frank R.), J., on 10 April 2000 in Superior Court, Wilson County, upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder. On 15 May 2001, the Supreme Court allowed defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to his appeal of an additional judgment.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Superior Court, Wilson County (North Carolina), of first-degree murder on the basis of premeditation and deliberation and under the felony murder rule, and attempted robbery with a dangerous weapon. He was sentenced to death and appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant murdered a cashier. The supreme court held he was not entitled to a venue change for pretrial publicity, individual voir dire, or a challenge for cause. His voir dire was not unduly limited, and he could not examine the jury about life sentence parole eligibility. He showed no basis for his objection to deoxyribonucleic acid (DNA) evidence. A photograph of him wearing a shirt found near the crime scene was relevant. His attempt to have lay witnesses vouch for another witness's veracity was improper questioning, as was his question seeking a hearsay response. The evidence of his premeditation and deliberation, as well as of his overt act in furtherance of robbery, was sufficient. The trial court did not have to intervene ex mero motu in the prosecutor's argument nor instruct the jury on a mitigating circumstance subsumed in another mitigating instruction. Common law robbery was inherently violent, so the aggravating instruction that he committed a violent crime did not err. As the evidence supported both premeditated and felony murder, the underlying felony could be an aggravating circumstance under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(e)(5). His sentence was proportional.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction and sentence were affirmed.

State v. Mann


No. 362A97


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


355 N.C. 294; 560 S.E.2d 776; 2002 N.C. LEXIS 332


December 12, 2001, Heard In the Supreme Court  
April 5, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Writ of certiorari denied: Mann v. North Carolina, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 8156 (U.S. Nov. 4, 2002).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal as of right pursuant to N. C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Stephens (Donald W.), J., on 15 July 1997 in Superior Court, Wake County, upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder. On 29 March 1999, the Supreme Court allowed defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to his appeal of additional judgments.



DISPOSITION: NO. 95CRS100098, FIRST-DEGREE MURDER: NO ERROR; NO. 95CRS100097, ROBBERY WITH A DANGEROUS WEAPON, AND 95CRS99884, CREDIT CARD THEFT: JUDGMENT VACATED AND REMANDED FOR RESENTENCING.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A Superior Court, Wake County, North Carolina, judgment, after a jury verdict, convicted defendant of first-degree murder upon the theory of felony murder, financial transaction card theft, and robbery with a dangerous weapon, and, after a N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000 proceeding jury recommendation, entered the death penalty for the murder and prison sentences for the non-capital crimes. Defendant appealed under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant's direct appeal of right to the supreme court under § 7A-27(a) in the capital case bypassed the appeals court. Defendant was laid off work, asked a co-worker to explain his unemployment benefits, took the co-worker to his home and beat her, took her and her credit cards and withdrew money or made unauthorized charges, put the co-worker in the trunk of his car, drove to a secluded area, shot her, discarded her body, and cleaned up blood in his apartment, all with his wife's help. The superior court arrested the conviction of kidnapping as part of the murder. The supreme court found sufficient evidence for the jury to find defendant guilty of all charges and to find N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(e)(5), (6), and (9) aggravating factors (during a robbery, for pecuniary gain, and especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel). The prosecutor's remarks were not beyond the fair comment scope. The jury, upon conviction, did not decide Edmund-Tison culpability, so collateral estoppel did not prevent that jury instruction during the sentencing phase. The death penalty was not disproportionate based upon comparison with other supreme court death penalty cases.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed that part of the judgment (1) convicting defendant of the crimes, and (2) sentencing defendant to death for first-degree murder. The supreme court reversed that part of the judgment sentencing defendant to prison for the two non-capital crimes, since there was insufficient evidence of the violation of trust or confidence aggravator, and remanded for re-sentencing for only the two non-capital crimes.

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal as of right pursuant to N. C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Barnette, J., on 13 July 1999 in Superior Court, Harnett County, upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder. DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Harnett County Superior Court (North Carolina) of first degree murder, first degree kidnapping, and robbery with a dangerous weapon and was sentenced to death. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant kidnapped the victim, killed him, and stole his car. The supreme court held defendant's confession was voluntary. The evidence allowed submission of more than one aggravating circumstance, and did not require submission of no prior criminal activity, mental disturbance, capacity to appreciate, and age mitigating circumstances. The death sentence was proportional.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction and sentence were affirmed.

State v. Anthony


No. 183A00


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


354 N.C. 372; 555 S.E.2d 557; 2001 N.C. LEXIS 1222


March 12, 2001, Heard In the Supreme Court  
December 18, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Seay, J., on 3 June 1999 in Superior Court, Gaston County, upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder. On 3 August 2000, the Supreme Court allowed defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to his appeal of an additional judgment.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court, Gaston County (North Carolina), upon a jury verdict, found defendant guilty of first-degree murder on the basis of malice, premeditation, and deliberation, but not on the basis of felony murder, and also guilty of assault with a deadly weapon with intent to kill inflicting serious injury. He was sentenced to death for the murder and to imprisonment for the assault. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant married and had children. He and his wife separated and he was violent towards her. She told others she feared him. She got an ex parte domestic violence protective order. She told her parents no longer to let him see the children before school. The day before the order hearing, he forced his way past her father to visit the children and became emotional. Later he got his shotgun, returned to her parents' place and shot her, then reloaded and shot her again as she pleaded for her life. He sped away, but later had someone drove him to police, to whom he confessed, and who found medication in his pocket. The supreme court held that defendant failed to show actual conflict of interest as to public defenders initially assigned to him who later joined the district attorney. His requests to rehabilitate jurors initially equivocal on the death penalty but later resolutely against it were properly denied. The victim's expressions of fear were "existing mental condition" hearsay exceptions; her expressions of concern for her children, while dying, were "excited utterances." An officer's testimony that he had probable cause for arrest was not a forbidden "interpretation of law."


OUTCOME: Defendant's trial and capital sentencing proceeding were free from prejudicial error and his sentence of death was not disproportionate.

State v. Fletcher


No. 117A96-2


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


354 N.C. 455; 555 S.E.2d 534; 2001 N.C. LEXIS 1226

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal as of right by defendant pursuant to N. C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a 9 December 1999 judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Lamm, J., at a resentencing proceeding held in Superior Court, Rutherford County, upon defendant's conviction of first-degree murder.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Rutherford County Superior Court (North Carolina) of first degree murder, first degree burglary, and robbery with a dangerous weapon. Following a second capital sentencing proceeding, defendant was sentenced to death. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and an accomplice broke into the victim's home and murdered her.
